reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    1D depthwise separable convolution block: pointwise Conv1d (channel mixing), depthwise Conv1d (spatial),
    GELU activation, and residual addition. Used in genomic foundation models (Enformer) for efficient
    sequence processing of DNA/RNA.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(channels, expanded_channels, 1)
        self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size,
                                   padding=kernel_size // 2, groups=expanded_channels)
        self.activation = nn.GELU()
        self.project_back = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, channels, 1)

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        x = self.pointwise(x)
        x = self.depthwise(x)
        x = self.activation(x)
        x = self.project_back(x)
        x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
expanded_channels = 512
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 9

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size]

Pointwise Conv1d: `expanded_channels -> channels`, kernel_size=1 5. Residual addition The input shape is `[batch_size, channels, seq_len] = [32, 256, 4096]`. The dimensions are: - Batch size: 32 - Input channels: 256 - Expanded channels: 512 - Sequence length: 4096 - Kernel size: 9 This operation is memory bandwidth bound. Let me calculate the memory traffic: - Input: 32 * 256 * 4096 = 33.5M floats = 134 MB - Output: same - Intermediate activations: - After pointwise1: 32 * 512 * 4096 = 67.1M floats = 268 MB - After depthwise: 32 * 512 * 4096 = 67.1M floats = 268 MB - After GELU: 32 * 512 * 4096 = 67.1M floats = 268 MB - After project_back: 32 * 256 * 4096 = 33.5M floats = 134 MB - Weights: - pointwise1: 256 * 512 * 1 = 131K floats = 0.5 MB - depthwise: 512 * 9 = 4608 floats = 0.02 MB - project_back: 512 * 256 * 1 = 131K floats = 0.5 MB A fused kernel that does all operations in one pass with tiling would be ideal. We need to: 1. Load input tile 2. Compute pointwise1 on the fly 3. Compute depthwise on the fly 4. Apply GELU 5. Compute project_back on the fly 6. Add residual 7. Store output Since the depthwise conv has kernel_size=9 with padding=4, each output position depends on 9 input positions. If we tile by sequence length, we need halo regions. For a fused kernel, I could process the entire sequence for a subset of batch elements and output channels. But given expanded_channels=512 and depthwise is groups=512, each output channel only depends on one input channel. Let me think about the data flow: - Input: [B, C, L] where C=256, L=4096 - Pointwise1: [B, C, L] -> [B, E, L] where each output position e,l = sum_c input[b,c,l] * pw1[e,c] - Depthwise: [B, E, L] -> [B, E, L] where each output position e,l = sum_k=0..8 input_depth[b,e,l-4+k] * dw[e,k] - GELU: elementwise - Project back: [B, E, L] -> [B, C, L] where each output position c,l = sum_e gelu[b,e,l] * pw2[c,e] - Add residual: + input[b,c,l] For fusion, processing one output position (b, c, l) at a time: - Compute all e channels' intermediate values for this (b, l): - For each e: compute pointwise1 = sum_c input[b,c,l] * pw1[e,c] - For each e: compute depthwise = sum_k=0..8 (sum_c input[b,c,l-4+k] * pw1[e,c]) * dw[e,k] - Apply GELU to each e - Compute output c,l = sum_e gelu_e * pw2[c,e] + input[b,c,l] Wait, the depthwise depends on neighboring l positions. If we compute output for (b,c,l), we need the pointwise1 results for positions l-4 to l+4. So we can't just compute pointwise1 for one l and then depthwise for the same l - we need the neighborhood. Option 1: Tile in L dimension. Process a block of L positions together. For each block, compute pointwise1 for the block plus halo, then depthwise, GELU, project_back, add residual. Option 2: Use shared memory. But we need to actually achieve this. For practical kernel design on H100, I should: 1. Use large tiles to maximize arithmetic intensity 2. Keep data in shared memory as much as possible 3. Use vectorized loads/stores 4. Minimize register pressure Since L=4096, if I tile by L=256, I get 4096/256 = 16 tiles per sequence. With batch=32, total blocks = 32 * 16 = 512 blocks. This might not fully saturate the GPU. Each block does significant work (256 channels * 256 positions * 512 expanded / some factor). Wait, each block should do enough work. Let's calculate work per block with L_tile=256: - pointwise1: 512 expanded * (256+8) positions * 256 channels = 34.4M multiply-adds - depthwise: 512 * 256 * 9 = 1.18M multiply-adds - project_back: 256 channels * 256 positions * 512 expanded = 33.5M multiply-adds - Total: ~69M multiply-adds per block At 512 blocks: 69M * 512 = 35.3B multiply-adds. This is roughly correct. Time at 67 TFLOPS: 35.3B / 67T = 0.53ms. Time at 3.35 TB/s with 270MB traffic: 0.08ms. So this is compute bound, not memory bound! The fused version becomes compute bound because we eliminated the memory traffic but kept the compute. To go faster, we need to utilize the compute efficiently. If we can use Tensor Cores for the pointwise convolutions (which are GEMMs essentially), we could go much faster. For pointwise1: for each l, it's a matrix-vector multiplication. But across l, it's a batched matrix-vector or a single GEMM if we reshape. - input: [B, C, L] -> reshape to [B*L, C] - pw1: [E, C] - output: [B*L, E] - This is GEMM: [B*L, C] x [C, E] = [B*L, E] For project_back: - gelu: [B, E, L] -> [B*L, E] - pw2: [C, E] -> [E, C] or [C, E] - output: [B*L, C] Both are GEMMs! The depthwise conv is separable per channel. On H100, using cuBLAS or CUTLASS for these GEMMs would be very fast. But we need to fuse everything. Alternative approach: use a custom kernel that leverages the H100 tensor cores for the GEMM-like parts. But maybe for a simpler approach, a well-optimized element-wise fused kernel with shared memory tiling can still be competitive, especially if the reference uses cuDNN with separate kernel launches and memory round-trips. Actually, looking at the problem again, the reference likely runs in ~0.5-2ms. A fused kernel that avoids the intermediate global memory roundtrips should be significantly faster. Let me think about the simplest practical fused kernel: ### Simpler approach: thread per (b, c, l) or cooperative blocks Actually, since depthwise is per-channel in the expanded space, another way to think about it: - For each output position (b, c, l), the value is: - sum_{e=0}^{511} pw2[c,e] * GELU( sum_{k=0}^{8} dw[e,k] * (sum_{c'=0}^{255} input[b,c',l-4+k] * pw1[e,c']) ) This is a single formula. If we expand: - output[b,c,l] = input[b,c,l] + sum_e pw2[c,e] * GELU( sum_k dw[e,k] * dot(pw1[e,:], input[b,:,l-4+k]) ) Let me define intermediate_m[b,e,l,k] = dot(pw1[e,:], input[b,:,l-4+k]) Then depthwise[b,e,l] = sum_k dw[e,k] * intermediate_m[b,e,l,k] Then output[b,c,l] = input[b,c,l] + sum_e pw2[c,e] * GELU(depthwise[b,e,l]) The key insight: for a fixed (b, e, l), intermediate_m[b,e,l,k] for k=0..8 is just 9 dot products of pw1[e,:] with input[b,:,l-4+k]. For fixed (b, e), as l varies, intermediate_m[b,e,l,:] is just sliding window dot products. For the fused kernel, let me try a tile-based approach where each block processes a tile of (b, c, l). ### Kernel Design v2 Grid: (batch, c_blocks, l_blocks) - batch = 32 - c_blocks = 256 / C_TILE - l_blocks = 4096 / L_TILE Within each block, threads compute a C_TILE x L_TILE output region. But the output for different c's share the same depthwise/GELU results. So we should compute the depthwise/GELU results once for all c's. Strategy: - Block computes all E=512 channels for a tile of L positions (plus halo), then computes all C=256 channels for those L positions. - Grid: (batch, num_l_blocks) - Block: compute for all C and some L tile. With 256 threads per block, and we need to compute 256 * L_TILE output elements. Let's try L_TILE = 64: - Output elements: 256 * 64 = 16384 - Threads: 256. Each thread computes 64 output elements? No, we need to parallelize better. Actually, we can have more threads! H100 allows up to 1024 threads per block. Let's use 512 threads. - Block: 512 threads. Each thread computes some (c, l) pairs. - With 256 * 64 = 16384 outputs and 512 threads: each thread computes 32 outputs. - We could have each thread handle 1 c and 32 l's, or 2 c's and 16 l's, etc. For SMEM: - We compute all E=512 channels for L_TILE + 8 = 72 positions. - To save SMEM, we can process E in chunks: E_TILE = 64. - For each E_TILE: 1. Compute pointwise1 for E_TILE x 72 positions: needs pw1[E_TILE, 256] and input[256, 72] - Store result in SMEM: 64 * 72 = 4608 floats = 18KB 2. Threads accumulate pw2[c, E_TILE] * gelu[E_TILE, l] for their assigned outputs - Each thread needs to dot 64 elements for each of its output positions - Total per thread: 32 positions * 64 elements = 2048 multiply-adds per E_TILE - For 8 E_TILEs: 16384 multiply-adds per thread 5. But 512 / 72 = 7.1, doesn't divide evenly. Alternative: use 256 threads for pointwise1. We have 64 e's * 72 l's = 4608 elements to compute. - Each element is a dot product of 256 values. - We can parallelize by assigning each thread to compute some (e, l) pairs. - 4608 elements / 256 threads = 18 elements per thread. - Each element = 256 MACs, so 18 * 256 = 4608 MACs per thread. - This fits well! So: 256 threads for pointwise1 compute, same 256 threads for accumulation. But we can use 512 threads to have more parallelism. Actually, let's use a simpler thread assignment: - Block has 256 threads - For pointwise1: thread t computes all (e, l) where (e * 72 + l) % 256 == t. So each thread computes ~18 elements. - For accumulation: thread t computes all (c, l) where (c * 64 + l) % 256 == t... hmm, this gets messy. Let me use a straightforward 2D assignment. Thread layout for 256 threads: (16, 16) or treat as 1D. For pointwise1 with 64 e and 72 l: - Map threads as e = threadIdx.x / 72? No, 256 < 64*72. - Better: each thread computes a contiguous chunk. - thread t computes elements with linear index from t * chunk to (t+1) * chunk. - chunk = 4608 / 256 = 18 elements per thread. - Element idx -> e = idx / 72, l = idx % 72. - Thread t handles elements [t*18, (t+1)*18). Similarly for output accumulation with 256 c and 64 l: - 16384 elements / 256 = 64 per thread. - thread t handles output elements [t*64, (t+1)*64). - c = idx / 64, l = idx % 64. This is simple! Let me verify the math works out. Actually, I need to be careful about the depthwise convolution. For pointwise1, we compute all e and all l in [block_start, block_end+8). Then for depthwise, each output at (e, l) for l in [block_start, block_end) is: sum_{k=0}^{8} pointwise1[e, l-4+k] * dw[e, k] Where the indexing is relative to the block. If block_start = block_idx * 64, then l ranges from block_start to block_start+63. For l = block_start, we need pointwise1 from block_start-4 to block_start+4. Some of these are in the previous block's domain. Since we're computing within one block, we'll compute pointwise1 for positions [block_start-4, block_start+64+3] = [block_start-4, block_start+67]. Wait, block_end = block_start + 64. For the last output position block_start+63, we need up to block_start+67. So the halo is [-4, 67] relative to block_start, which is 72 positions. For l = block_start, input positions are l-4 to l+4. With padding=4, for l < 4, some inputs are padded with 0. The original code uses `padding=kernel_size // 2 = 4`, so for l from 0 to 4095: - For l < 4: valid input starts from l-4+4 = l, wait no, with padding=4, the conv output has the same length as input because the padding fills the boundaries. For l=0, the kernel positions are at [-4, -3, -2, -1, 0, 1, 2, 3, 4]. Let me lay out the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv_gelu_residual( const float* input, const float* pw1_weight, // [expanded_channels, channels] const float* dw_weight, // [expanded_channels, kernel_size] const float* pw2_weight, // [channels, expanded_channels] float* output, int batch_size, int channels, int expanded_channels, int seq_len, int kernel_size, int padding ) { const int L = seq_len; const int C = channels; const int E = expanded_channels; const int K = kernel_size; const int P = padding; const int L_TILE = 128; const int E_TILE = 64; int b = blockIdx.x; int l_block = blockIdx.y; if (b >= batch_size) return; int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; int l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L); int l_count = l_end - l_start; int l_win_start = l_start - P; // l_start - 4 int l_win_end = l_end + P - 1; // l_end + 3 int l_win_count = l_win_end - l_win_start + 1; // l_count + 8 = 136 for full blocks // Shared memory layout // smem_pw1: E_TILE x l_win_count // smem_dw: E_TILE x l_count (after depthwise + GELU) extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_pw1 = smem; // E_TILE * l_win_count float* smem_dw = smem + E_TILE * l_win_count; // E_TILE * l_count // Each thread's output accumulation // Thread t computes output for some (c, l) pairs int num_outputs = C * l_count; int outputs_per_thread = (num_outputs + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int out_start = threadIdx.x * outputs_per_thread; int out_end = min(out_start + outputs_per_thread, num_outputs); // Registers for output accumulation float out_vals[128 / (512/256)]; // max 128 outputs per thread... actually 64 for 512 threads and 32768 outputs. // For simplicity, we can process dynamically or use shared memory for output accumulation. ``` Actually, I'm overcomplicating the register allocation. Actually, I realize I can process the entire problem more efficiently if I think about it differently. The operation is: out = pw2 @ GELU( depthwise_conv1d( pw1 @ input ) ) + input Where @ denotes appropriate matrix operations. If I process per batch element and per sequence position tile: - For positions [l_start, l_end), load input[:, l_start-4:l_end+4) - Compute pw1 @ input_chunk -> intermediate (E x chunk) - Depthwise conv on intermediate -> depthwise_out (E x L_tile) - GELU -> gelu_out (E x L_tile) - pw2 @ gelu_out -> output_chunk (C x L_tile) - Add residual -> store This is exactly what I described. Let me just code it up. For the CUDA kernel, I need to be careful with: 1. Padding handling 2. Grid/block dimensions 3. Shared memory management 4. Synchronization Let me draft the kernel: ```cuda template <int L_TILE, int E_TILE> __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ pw1, // [E, C] const float* __restrict__ dw, // [E, K] const float* __restrict__ pw2, // [C, E] float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K ) { const int b = blockIdx.x; const int l_block = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B) return; const int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; const int l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L); const int l_count = l_end - l_start; const int P = K / 2; const int l_win_start = l_start - P; const int l_win_end = l_end + P - 1; const int l_win_count = l_end - l_start + K - 1; // = l_count + 8 for K=9 // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_pw1 = smem; // E_TILE * l_win_count - stores pointwise1 output float* smem_out = smem; // Reuse: C * l_count - stores accumulated output // But we need both simultaneously during accumulation? No! // We can reuse smem_pw1 after computing depthwise. // Actually: compute pointwise1 -> store in smem_pw1 // compute depthwise -> store back in first part or different part // accumulate to output_smem. // Since l_win_count >= l_count, and E_TILE < C usually, we need to plan carefully. // Better: // smem_pw1: E_TILE * l_win_count // smem_dw: E_TILE * l_count (can overlap with part of smem_pw1 since l_win_count >= l_count) // But let's keep it simple with separate allocations. } ``` Actually, for L_TILE=128, l_win_count=136. For E_TILE=64: - smem_pw1 = 64 * 136 = 8704 floats - smem_dw = 64 * 128 = 8192 floats - smem_out = 256 * 128 = 32768 floats Total = 8704 + 8192 + 32768 = 49664 floats = 198KB. Under 227KB! For the first two, we can actually reuse memory because we don't need smem_pw1 after computing smem_dw. But we need to be careful about the sizes: smem_pw1 is larger (8704) than smem_dw (8192). We can store smem_dw at the start of smem, overwriting smem_pw1 as we compute. But if we want accumulation to happen while smem_dw is valid, and then we overwrite it for the next E_tile, that's fine. The accumulation reads smem_dw and updates smem_out. So: - smem: first part = smem_pw1 (size E_TILE * l_win_count) - During depthwise, we can write to the same memory or reuse the first E_TILE * l_count. - smem_out starts after smem_pw1. Total = E_TILE * l_win_count + C * l_count. For E_TILE=64, l_win_count=136, C=256, l_count=128: = 64*136 + 256*128 = 8704 + 32768 = 41472 floats = 165KB. Very comfortable. But wait, during depthwise we might overwrite smem_pw1 values that are still needed for neighboring positions! The depthwise for position (e, l) needs l-4 to l+4 from smem_pw1. If we compute depthwise in place (only using valid positions), we need to be careful about the order. We should compute from left to right so that we don't overwrite values still needed by positions to the right. For a given e, depthwise at l needs pw1[e, l-4] to pw1[e, l+4]. If we compute l=0 first, it needs indices -4 to 4 (in the window, indices 0 to 8). For l=1, it needs indices -3 to 5 (window indices 1 to 9). ... For l=127, it needs indices 123 to 131 (window indices 127 to 135). Since the output indices are always >= the earliest input index, and we're reading from the window and writing to output positions, we can write the output to a separate array. We can store smem_dw at a different location: - smem_pw1: offset 0, size E_TILE * l_win_count - smem_dw: offset E_TILE * l_win_count, size E_TILE * l_count Wait, but then total is E_TILE * l_win_count + E_TILE * l_count + C * l_count = 8704 + 8192 + 32768 = 49664 = 198KB. Still under 227KB. Actually no, C * l_count comes after. Let me just use three buffers: - smem_pw1 at 0 - smem_dw at E_TILE * l_win_count - smem_out at E_TILE * l_win_count + E_TILE * l_count Total = 49664 floats = 198KB. Fine. For L_TILE=256: - l_win_count = 264 - smem_pw1 = 64 * 264 = 16896 - smem_dw = 64 * 256 = 16384 - smem_out = 256 * 256 = 65536 - Total = 98816 floats = 395KB. Too much! So L_TILE=128 is the max if we store output in SMEM. What if we store output in registers? - Each thread accumulates its assigned outputs in registers, then writes to global memory at the end. - This eliminates smem_out, reducing SMEM to 8704 + 8192 = 16896 floats = 68KB. - Then we can increase L_TILE or threads. With L_TILE=256 and no output SMEM: - Total outputs = 256 * 256 = 65536. - With 1024 threads: 64 per thread. - Registers: 64 floats = 256 bytes per thread. Plus some temps. Total ~1KB per thread. - 1024 threads * 1KB = 1MB total registers. Per SM: if we run 1 block of 1024 threads, that's 1MB. SM has 64K*4=256KB registers. So 1024 threads with 64 floats = 64K registers. At 255 max per thread: 1024*255 = 261K registers > 256K limit. So we need fewer registers per thread. - 64 output floats + ~32 temp = 96 floats per thread. 1024 threads = 96K registers > 256K/SM limit! (96K * 4 bytes = 384KB > 256KB per SM) So we can't use 1024 threads with 96 registers each. We need fewer threads or fewer registers. Let's use 512 threads. Each handles 65536/512 = 128 outputs. 128 register floats = 512 bytes per thread. 512 threads * 128 = 65K floats = 260KB. Close to 256KB/SM limit. Plus temps: too much. Let's use 256 threads. Each handles 256 outputs. 256 register floats = 1KB per thread. 256 threads = 256KB. Still likely too much for the SM register file. So L_TILE=128 with register accumulation is safer. Each thread handles 32768/512 = 64 outputs. 64 floats = 256 bytes per thread. 512 threads * 64 = 32K floats = 128KB. Plus other temps, comfortably under 256KB/SM. With L_TILE=128, threads=512, register accumulation: - SMEM: smem_pw1 + smem_dw = 8704 + 8192 = 16896 floats = 68KB. - Very comfortable. Let's go with L_TILE=128, E_TILE=64, threads=512. ### Final Kernel Design Grid: (batch, num_l_blocks) = (32, 32) since 4096/128 = 32. Block: 512 threads, ~68KB SMEM. Per block: 1. Initialize output accumulators: each thread has `out[64]` in registers, initialized to 0. 2. For e_tile = 0 to 7 (since 512/64 = 8): a. Compute pointwise1 for e in [e_tile*64, (e_tile+1)*64) and l_win in [0, 136): - Store in smem_pw1[e_local, l_win] b. __syncthreads() c. Compute depthwise for e_local in [0, 64) and l in [0, 128): - Read from smem_pw1, apply kernel, write to smem_dw[l, e_local] d. __syncthreads() e. Apply GELU to smem_dw f. __syncthreads() g. Accumulate into output registers: - Each thread reads its assigned (c, l) pairs. - For each pair, dot product of pw2[c, e_tile*64:(e_tile+1)*64] with smem_dw[l, :] h. __syncthreads() 3. After all e_tiles: - Load input residual for each assigned output position - Add residual to accumulator - Write to output global memory For thread assignment: - Pointwise1: 64 * 136 = 8704 elements. 512 threads -> 17 elements each. - linear_idx = threadIdx.x * 17 + i (i from 0 to 16) - e_local = linear_idx / 136 - l_win = linear_idx % 136 - Some threads may have fewer elements if 8704 doesn't divide evenly by 512. - Actually 8704 / 512 = 17 exactly. Perfect! - Depthwise: 128 * 64 = 8192 elements. 512 threads -> 16 elements each. - linear_idx = threadIdx.x * 16 + i - l = linear_idx / 64 - e_local = linear_idx % 64 - Again 8192 / 512 = 16 exactly. - Accumulation and output: 256 * 128 = 32768 elements. 512 threads -> 64 elements each. - linear_idx = threadIdx.x * 64 + i - c = linear_idx / 128 - l = linear_idx % 128 - 32768 / 512 = 64 exactly. This is beautifully divisible! One concern: pointwise1 needs input[b, :, l_actual] for each (e_local, l_win). If a thread handles 17 elements with potentially 17 different l_win values, it needs to load input for 17 different positions. Each position requires 256 floats (all channels). That's a lot of loads per thread (17 * 256 = 4352 loads). But input for the same l_win is reused across many threads. Specifically, for a given l_win, there are 64 e_local values. The 64 threads handling this l_win will all load the same input slice. This is great for caching! Wait, linear_idx = e_local * 136 + l_win. So for a fixed l_win, e_local varies. If thread t handles elements [t*17, (t+1)*17): - e_local = base / 136, l_win = base % 136 - For the next element: e_local increases if l_win wraps. - So a thread's 17 elements might span different e_local and l_win. Actually, let's trace thread 0: elements 0 to 16. - Element 0: e=0, l=0 - Element 1: e=0, l=1 - ... - Element 16: e=0, l=16 Thread 1: elements 17 to 33. - Element 17: e=0, l=17 - ... - Element 33: e=0, l=33 Wait, 17 elements per thread, 136 l_win values. So thread t covers a contiguous chunk of l_win for a specific e_local... no. - Thread 0: l_win = 0..16, e_local = 0 - Thread 1: l_win = 17..33, e_local = 0 - ... - Thread 8: l_win = 136..152? But l_win max is 135. So 136/8 = 17, thread 7: l_win = 119..135, e_local = 0. - Thread 8: element 136 = e_local=1, l_win=0. Oh I see: thread 0 to 7 cover e_local=0, l_win=0..135 (in chunks of 17). Thread 8 to 15 cover e_local=1, etc. This is perfect! For e_local=0, threads 0-7 load input for l_win=0..135. They all load the SAME input channels but for different l_win. The input for different l_win is contiguous in memory (since l is the innermost dimension). So threads 0-7 have coalesced loads for input! For the weights pw1: thread 0 loads pw1[0, :] (256 floats), thread 8 loads pw1[64, :]? No wait, e_local=1 means e_global = e_tile*64 + 1. Thread 8 needs pw1[e_global, :] = pw1[e_tile*64 + 1, :]. Thread 0 needs pw1[e_tile*64, :]. These are separated by 256 floats = 1KB. Not perfectly coalesced between thread 0 and 8, but within a warp there might be some threads using the same e_local. Actually, threads 0-7 all use e_local=0 (e_global = e_tile*64). They load the SAME pw1 row. That's good for cache but bad for bank conflicts if done through SMEM. But we're reading pw1 from global memory, and with L1 cache, the first thread to read a row will cache it for the others. Wait, 8 threads in a warp all reading the same 1KB memory block. A single warp can broadcast this through the L1 cache. So it's actually very efficient! Let me verify: threads 0-7 in warp 0 all process e_local=0 with consecutive l_win. They all need pw1[e_global, :] (same weights, 256 floats). The first load will fetch the cache line, and subsequent loads hit L1. Great! For input: thread t processes l_win = t*17 + i. For t=0, l_win=0..16. For t=1, l_win=17..33. These are contiguous in memory. With 32 threads in a warp, they load 32*17 = 544 consecutive l_win values... but wait, thread 0 loads input[:, 0] to input[:, 16] (17 different slices), thread 1 loads input[:, 17] to input[:, 33], etc. For a fixed i (say i=0), threads 0-31 load input[:, 0], input[:, 17], input[:, 34], ... These are strided by 17 floats = 68 bytes. Hmm, that's not great coalescing. Let me reconsider the assignment. Alternative assignment for pointwise1: - Make e_local inner and l_win outer? - Or assign threads such that nearby threads work on nearby memory. Since input[b, c, l] is contiguous in l, we want nearby threads to access nearby l. If we use l_win as the fast (inner) dimension and e_local as outer: - 512 threads, each handles 17 elements. - element_idx = threadIdx.x * 17 + i - l_win = element_idx / 64 - e_local = element_idx % 64 Thread 0: l_win=0, e_local=0..16 Thread 1: l_win=0, e_local=17..33 ... Thread 3: l_win=0, e_local=51..63 Thread 4: l_win=1, e_local=0..16 This is worse because threads 0-3 all load input[:, 0] (same l), but different e. The l coalescing is bad. Let me go back to the original but think about warps. With 512 threads, there are 16 warps. Each warp has 32 threads. In the original assignment: element_idx = threadIdx.x * 17 + i e_local = element_idx / 136 l_win = element_idx % 136 Warp 0 (threads 0-31): - Thread 0: e=0, l=0..16 - Thread 1: e=0, l=17..33 - Thread 2: e=0, l=34..50 - Thread 3: e=0, l=51..67 - Thread 4: e=0, l=68..84 - Thread 5: e=0, l=85..101 - Thread 6: e=0, l=102..118 - Thread 7: e=0, l=119..135 - Thread 8: e=1, l=0..16 - Thread 9: e=1, l=17..33 - ... - Thread 15: e=1, l=119..135 - Thread 16: e=2... - Thread 31: e=3, l=102..118 For a fixed i=0: - Thread 0: l=0 - Thread 1: l=17 - Thread 2: l=34 - ... - Thread 7: l=119 - Thread 8: l=0 (same as thread 0!) - Thread 9: l=17 - ... - Thread 31: l=102 So within the warp, for i=0: - L values accessed: 0, 17, 34, 51, 68, 85, 102, 119, 0, 17, 34, 51, 68, 85, 102, 119, ... - These are not contiguous, but they repeat. The first 8 threads and next 8 threads both access l=0, 17, etc. Actually, for l=0, threads 0, 8, 16, 24 access it (for e=0, 1, 2, 3 respectively). For l=0, they need input[b, :, 0]. All 4 threads need the same slice! This is great for L1 cache hit rate. For pw1 weights: thread 0 reads pw1[e_tile*64, :], thread 8 reads pw1[e_tile*64+1, :], etc. These are contiguous in memory (separated by 256 floats = 1KB). This is actually decent memory access. So maybe the original assignment is OK after all, because: 1. Multiple threads in a warp read the same input slice -> good cache reuse 2. Threads read pw1 weights for consecutive e -> somewhat coalesced through cache But 8 threads hitting the same l=0 input slice... if they all execute the i=0 iteration simultaneously, the first load will fetch from global memory, and the next 3 will hit L1. Very efficient! Actually, this might be even better than strict coalescing because we're reading the same cache line multiple times and hitting L1. Let me also consider an alternative where we process l_win in tiles: - For a chunk of l_win values (say 8), all threads compute all e_local for those l_win. - This would mean 512 threads computing 64 e_local * 8 l_win = 512 elements. Each thread gets 1 element. - Each element needs 256 MACs. - For l_win chunk, all threads read from the same 8 input positions but different e weights. - This requires loading 8 * 256 = 2048 input floats into SMEM first! Then all threads read from SMEM. This is the "load input to SMEM, compute cooperatively" approach. But input is large: 256 * 136 = 34,816 floats = 139KB. That's a LOT of SMEM. Fits! But this is more complex to code. Maybe for a first implementation, I should stick with the direct approach. Actually, let me write the kernel with a simpler thread assignment for pointwise1. Use 256 threads (not 512) for pointwise1, and have each thread compute 34 elements (8704/256 = 34). Wait, all operations in a block must use the same thread count. We launch with 512 threads total. For GELU: I can use the PyTorch GELU approximation or exact formula. The reference nn.GELU uses the exact `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` by default. In practice, PyTorch's GELU has `approximate` parameter which defaults to `'none'`, meaning exact. But this is slow. Actually, I should check: the reference doesn't specify `approximate`, so it uses the exact formulation. For numerical equivalence, I need to match exactly. Wait, CUDA's `erf` function might not match PyTorch's exactly. But PyTorch's GELU uses some algorithm, and if we use `erff()` in CUDA, it should be very close. Let me check if standard `erff` matches PyTorch within fp32 tolerance. Actually, let me use the exact GELU formula with `erff()`: ```c inline float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865475f)); } ``` This should match PyTorch closely enough for fp32 tolerance (1e-4). Now, writing the kernel: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __inline__ __device__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865475f)); } template <int L_TILE, int E_TILE> __global__ void fused_conv_gelu_res_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ pw1_weight, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ pw2_weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K ) { const int b = blockIdx.x; const int l_block = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B) return; const int P = K / 2; const int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; const int l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L); const int l_count = l_end - l_start; const int l_win_start = l_start - P; const int l_win_end = l_end + P - 1; const int l_win_count = l_count + K - 1; // should be L_TILE + K - 1 for full blocks // Shared memory layout // smem_pw1: [E_TILE][l_win_count] // smem_dw: [l_count][E_TILE] (after depthwise) extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_pw1 = smem; // size E_TILE * l_win_count float* smem_dw = smem; // reuse: size E_TILE * l_count (<= E_TILE * l_win_count) // Actually, we can compute depthwise in-place into the first part of smem_pw1 // because for l_count positions, we only need E_TILE * l_count floats. // But depthwise at l needs l_win indices l to l+8. If we overwrite l_win=0 // when computing l=0, then the next computation that needs l_win=0 is... none! // l starts from 0 for this block, and l_win ranges from -4 to l_count+3. // For l=0, needs l_win indices mapped to 0..8. These correspond to actual positions -4..4. // We can safely overwrite them after all computations that need them are done. // But computing l in increasing order, l=1 needs l_win indices 1..9, // so when we compute l=0 and store at index 0, we must not overwrite l_win indices 1..8 yet. // If we write depthwise result to a separate buffer, we have no issues. // Use separate buffers to be safe float* smem_depthwise = smem + E_TILE * l_win_count; // Thread output accumulation const int num_out = C * l_count; const int out_per_thread = (num_out + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int out_start = threadIdx.x * out_per_thread; const int out_end = min(out_start + out_per_thread, num_out); // Accumulate output in registers float acc[64]; // Max 64 outputs per thread for 512 threads and 32768 outputs #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) acc[i] = 0.0f; int num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE; for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < num_e_tiles; e_tile++) { int e_base = e_tile * E_TILE; // --- Pointwise1 --- // Compute pw1 output for this E_TILE and all l_win positions const int pw1_total = E_TILE * l_win_count; const int pw1_per_thread = (pw1_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int pw1_start = threadIdx.x * pw1_per_thread; const int pw1_end = min(pw1_start + pw1_per_thread, pw1_total); for (int idx = pw1_start; idx < pw1_end; idx++) { int e_local = idx / l_win_count; int l_win = idx % l_win_count; int e_global = e_base + e_local; int l_actual = l_win_start + l_win; float sum = 0.0f; if (l_actual >= 0 && l_actual < L) { const float* inp_ptr = input + ((b * C + 0) * L + l_actual); const float* w_ptr = pw1_weight + (e_global * C + 0); #pragma unroll 4 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum += w_ptr[c] * inp_ptr[c * L]; } } smem_pw1[e_local * l_win_count + l_win] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // --- Depthwise --- const int dw_total = E_TILE * l_count; const int dw_per_thread = (dw_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int dw_start = threadIdx.x * dw_per_thread; const int dw_end = min(dw_start + dw_per_thread, dw_total); for (int idx = dw_start; idx < dw_end; idx++) { int l = idx / E_TILE; int e_local = idx % E_TILE; int e_global = e_base + e_local; float sum = 0.0f; // Convolve over kernel_size=9 for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int l_win = l + k; // because smem_pw1 stores from l_win_start = l_start - P, // and for output l, the center is at l, which corresponds to // l_win index = (l_start + l) - l_win_start = l_start + l - (l_start - P) = l + P // Wait no. l_win_start = l_start - P. // For output position l_global = l_start + l, we need input positions l_global - P to l_global + P. // In smem_pw1, these are stored at l_win indices: // l_global - P - l_win_start = l_start + l - P - (l_start - P) = l // to l_global + P - l_win_start = l + 2*P = l + K - 1 // So the window for output l starts at smem_pw1 index l. sum += smem_pw1[e_local * l_win_count + (l + k)] * dw_weight[e_global * K + k]; } smem_depthwise[l * E_TILE + e_local] = gelu(sum); } __syncthreads(); // --- Accumulate project_back --- // Each thread handles some (c, l) pairs for (int out_idx = out_start; out_idx < out_end; out_idx++) { int c = out_idx / l_count; int l = out_idx % l_count; int acc_i = out_idx - out_start; float sum = acc[acc_i]; const float* w_ptr = pw2_weight + (c * E + e_base); const float* act_ptr = smem_depthwise + (l * E_TILE); #pragma unroll 4 for (int e_local = 0; e_local < E_TILE && (e_base + e_local) < E; e_local++) { sum += w_ptr[e_local] * act_ptr[e_local]; } acc[acc_i] = sum; } __syncthreads(); } // --- Add residual and write output --- for (int out_idx = out_start; out_idx < out_end; out_idx++) { int c = out_idx / l_count; int l = out_idx % l_count; int acc_i = out_idx - out_start; int l_global = l_start + l; float res = input[((b * C + c) * L + l_global)]; float val = acc[acc_i] + res; output[((b * C + c) * L + l_global)] = val; } } ``` Wait, I used `acc[64]` which is a variable-length array based on runtime value. I should use a fixed max size. Actually, since `out_per_thread` depends on blockDim.x and constants, and for known blockDim.x=512, num_out=C*l_count, but l_count is runtime variable (can be < 128 for boundary blocks). For safety, I can use dynamic indexing with a fixed-size array. But since C=256 and l_count<=128, max num_out=32768. With blockDim.x=512, out_per_thread=64. So an array of 64 is fine. Actually, I can't have `float acc[64];` if the actual needed size is smaller in some blocks. But we can just always use 64 and only compute up to `out_end - out_start` elements. Wait, for C=256, L=4096, L_TILE=128, l_count is always 128 because 4096 is divisible by 128. Same for boundary conditions... well the formula `l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L)` ensures correctness, but since 4096 / 128 = 32 exactly, l_count is always 128. For blockDim.x=512: 32768 / 512 = 64 exactly. So every thread computes exactly 64 outputs. One issue: `#pragma unroll 4` with loops where the trip count might not be divisible by 4. The compiler should handle this. Another issue: the depthwise convolution indexing. Let me verify: - `l_win_start = l_start - P = l_start - 4` - `l_win_count = l_count + K - 1 = 128 + 8 = 136` - `smem_pw1[e_local * l_win_count + l_win]` stores pointwise1 at actual position `l_win_start + l_win`. - For output position `l` (0-indexed within block, global = l_start + l): - Needs input positions from `l_start + l - 4` to `l_start + l + 4`. - In smem_pw1, these correspond to `l_win` indices from `(l_start + l - 4) - l_win_start = l` to `(l_start + l + 4) - l_win_start = l + 8`. - So `l_win = l + k` for k=0..8. Yes, my code has `smem_pw1[e_local * l_win_count + (l + k)]`. Correct! For `l + k` to be within [0, l_win_count): - l ranges [0, 127], k ranges [0, 8], so l+k ranges [0, 135]. - l_win_count = 136. So we're good. For the last block in a sequence where L is not divisible by L_TILE? Here 4096/128=32 exactly, so all blocks are full. But the code handles partial blocks via l_count. Let me also think about the memory access in the accumulation step: ```c const float* w_ptr = pw2_weight + (c * E + e_base); const float* act_ptr = smem_depthwise + (l * E_TILE); ``` - w_ptr reads pw2_weight[c, e_base:e_base+E_TILE], which is contiguous in memory (since pw2 is [C, E]). - act_ptr reads smem_depthwise[l, :], which is contiguous in SMEM (since we stored as [l_count, E_TILE]). This is great! One contiguous read from global (pw2) and one from SMEM (activation). For the inner loop: ```c for (int e_local = 0; e_local < E_TILE && (e_base + e_local) < E; e_local++) ``` Due to loop unrolling, if the compiler can't determine E_TILE is constant, the unroll might not work well. But E_TILE is a template parameter, so it should be fine. The condition `(e_base + e_local) < E` is for partial tiles at the boundary. Since E=512 and E_TILE=64, there are no partial tiles! We can simplify by removing the condition, or keep it for correctness with other sizes. Actually, `num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE = 8` for E=512, E_TILE=64. The last tile (e_base=448) has e_local=0..63, and e_base+63=511 < 512, so all are valid. The boundary condition is unnecessary but harmless. Let me think about occupancy. Shared memory is 68KB (for L_TILE=128, E_TILE=64, K=9). - 68KB per block. SM has 227KB, so it can fit 3 blocks (68*3=204KB) or 4 with some spilling. Actually, blocks of 512 threads each. - H100 SM can have up to 32 thread blocks simultaneously. But with 512 threads/block, that's 16K threads/SM max, while the hardware supports 2048 threads/SM. Wait, 2048/512 = 4 blocks per SM. - With 68KB SMEM, 227/68 = 3 blocks per SM due to SMEM. But 4 blocks for threads. So SMEM is the limiting factor. - 3 blocks per SM * 132 SMs * 512 threads = 202,752 concurrent threads. - Total blocks = 32*32 = 1024. 1024 / 3 = 342 SMs needed in one wave. But we only have 132. So ~3 waves. - 202K concurrent threads. This is a good amount for latency hiding. Wait, H100 has ~132 SMs? Actually H100 SXM has 132 SMs, H100 NVL has 132 or 144. At 3 blocks per SM: 396 blocks concurrently. Total 1024 / 396 = 2.6 waves. This should be fine. Now, let me think about register pressure. Each thread needs: - Loop variables: ~8 floats - acc array: 64 floats = 256 bytes - sum variables: ~4 floats = 16 bytes - Total: ~72 floats = 288 bytes = 72 32-bit registers. - But the compiler might use more due to loop unrolling and pipelining. If each thread uses ~96 registers: 512 threads * 96 = 49K registers per block. 256KB/SM / 64 bytes per register (wait, register file is 64K 32-bit registers per SM). - 49K registers for one block. 3 blocks = 147K < 256K. - 4 blocks = 196K < 256K. But SMEM limits to 3 blocks anyway. So register usage is fine. What if I want to optimize further? I could: 1. Use `__ldg` for read-only global memory (input and weights) 2. Use float4 for vectorized loads where possible 3. Tune block size and tile sizes 4. Use approximate GELU (tanh) if numerical tolerance allows For approximate GELU: PyTorch's default GELU (approximate='none') uses exact erf. If we use tanh approximation, it might not match within 1e-4 fp32 tolerance. Let me stick with exact. Could try `fast_math` with `erff`? `erff` in CUDA is already fairly optimized. For `__ldg`: The weights pw1 and pw2 can benefit from `__ldg` since they're read-only. Input and output too. ```c const float* __restrict__ input = ... // already have __restrict__ ``` `__ldg` is useful when doing non-coalesced reads that are broadcast or cached. For pw1 weights, each thread reads a row of 256 floats. These are contiguous within the thread's loop, so regular loads should already be efficient. Actually, since multiple threads read the same pw1 row, `__ldg` could help because it goes through the read-only data cache (which may be faster or have better broadcast properties than L1). SMEM banks on H100: I believe it's 32 banks, 4 bytes each. So a float accesses one bank. If pw1 is stored as [64, 256], and the first dimension is e_local: - Thread t reads pw1[e_local_t, c] for varying c. - For a fixed c, threads with different e_local access pw1[0, c], pw1[1, c], ..., pw1[31, c]. - These are addresses: base + 0*256 + c, base + 1*256 + c, ..., base + 31*256 + c. - Stride between consecutive e_local is 256 floats = 1024 bytes. - 1024 / (32*4) = 8 bank strides. So they access banks c % 32, (c+8) % 32, etc. Actually, the bank is (address / 4) % 32. - Address for e_local=i: (base + i*256 + c) * 4 bytes. Bank = ((base/4 + i*256 + c) % 32). - Since 256 % 32 = 0, bank = (base/4 + c) % 32 for all i! - ALL threads in a warp reading the same c will access the SAME bank! That's a 32-way bank conflict! To avoid this, we need to transpose pw1: store as [c, e_local] with c being the fast dimension that's contiguous. Address for c=i, e_local=j: base + i*64 + j. Bank = ((base/4 + i*64 + j) % 32). For j varying 0..31 and fixed i: banks are (base/4 + i*64) % 32 + j % 32. These are all different! No bank conflicts! So if we load pw1 into SMEM as [C, E_TILE] (column-major for the E dimension, i.e., transposed relative to global memory), we get conflict-free access. But loading from global to SMEM with transposition requires care: - Global pw1 is [E, C], so pw1[e, c] is at index e*C + c. - SMEM should be at index c*E_TILE + e_local. - Each thread can load a few elements and write to transposed positions. Similarly for pw2: global is [C, E]. For accumulation, threads read pw2[c, e] for fixed c and varying e. If stored in SMEM as [C, E_TILE], then reading pw2[c, :] is contiguous. But threads in the same warp with different c but same e access: - Thread t1 reads pw2[c1, e] at bank ((base/4 + c1*E_TILE + e) % 32) - Thread t2 reads pw2[c2, e] at bank ((base/4 + c2*E_TILE + e) % 32) - If E_TILE=64, bank difference between c1 and c2 is (c1-c2)*64 % 32 = 0. So ALL threads reading the same e but different c hit the same bank! For pw2, threads handling different c but the same e need pw2[c, e_global]. With E_TILE=64, the stride in SMEM between c rows is 64, which is a multiple of 32. This leads to bank conflicts. To avoid bank conflicts for pw2: - Store pw2 in SMEM as [E_TILE, C] with e being fast dimension. - Then pw2[e, c] is at index e*C + c. For fixed e, varying c is contiguous. For fixed c, varying e is strided by C=256. - Wait, if threads read pw2[:, c] for fixed c, with e varying: - Address = base + e*C + c. Bank = ((base/4 + e*256 + c) % 32) = ((base/4 + c) % 32) since 256 % 32 = 0. - All e for the same c hit the same bank! And since we use float offsets which are 4-byte aligned, `l*65*4 + e4*4*4` = 4*(l*65 + e4*4). For this to be 16-byte aligned, `l*65 + e4*4` must be divisible by 4. `e4*4` is divisible by 4, but `l*65` mod 4 = l*1 mod 4. So it's NOT always 16-byte aligned! But wait, I discovered earlier that E_TILE=64 leads to bank conflicts. E_TILE_PAD=68 would avoid bank conflicts (68/4=17, 17%32=17, diff=17 between rows. Adjacent e's in the same warp: bank diff = 68 % 32 = 4. No conflict!). Actually, let's verify bank conflicts for E_TILE_PAD=68: - Address = base + l*68*4 + e*4. For fixed l and e varying by 1: address diff = 4 bytes. Bank diff = 1. No conflict. - Wait, this is when reading one element per thread. If we read float4: address diff = 16 bytes, bank diff = 4. Still no conflict within 32 threads if they all read different e values. - For float4: 4 consecutive floats in the same row. These span 4 consecutive banks (since each float is 4 bytes = 1 bank). A single float4 per thread has no intra-thread bank conflict because it's 4 contiguous banks. - Across 32 threads reading 32 different float4's starting at e=0,4,8,...: each float4 uses 4 banks. 8 threads cover e=0..31 (8 float4s = 32 floats). These use 32 consecutive banks (0..31). Next 8 threads cover e=32..63, using banks 0..31 again! So threads 1 and 9 (reading e=4 and e=36) both use banks 4,5,6,7. Bank conflict! For float4 reads with stride 68 * 4 bytes between rows: - Thread 0 reads e=0: banks 0,1,2,3 - Thread 1 reads e=4: banks 4,5,6,7 - ... - Thread 7 reads e=28: banks 28,29,30,31 - Thread 8 reads e=32: banks 0,1,2,3 -> CONFLICT with thread 0! So float4 reads with 32 threads still have conflicts if more than 8 threads read from the same l and different e that wrap around 32 banks. To avoid this with float4, the stride between e rows should be coprime with 32 when divided by 16 bytes (4 floats). - Stride in bytes = E_TILE_PAD * 4. - Stride in float4 units = E_TILE_PAD. - We need E_TILE_PAD to be odd (so that after 32 threads, we don't repeat banks). - Wait, address = base + l*E_TILE_PAD*4 + e*4. - For float4 at e=4*i: address = base + l*E_TILE_PAD*4 + 4*i*4 = base + l*E_TILE_PAD*4 + 16*i. - Bank = (addr / 4) % 32 = ((base/4 + l*E_TILE_PAD + 4*i) % 32). - For i varying: bank = (const + 4*i) % 32. - This means consecutive i values differ by 4 in bank. - With 32 threads and i=0..31: banks covered are const, const+4, const+8, ..., const+124 ≡ const+28 (mod 32). - Banks covered: {const, const+4, const+8, const+12, const+16, const+20, const+24, const+28} mod 32. - Only 8 distinct banks! The remaining 24 banks are unused, and all 32 threads map to 8 banks, causing 4-way conflicts. So float4 reads with row-major [l, e] layout ALWAYS have bank conflicts if multiple threads read different rows with the same l. The degree of conflict depends on the access pattern. If E_TILE_PAD is a multiple of 32, then each float4 starts at the same bank for all rows. That causes 32-way conflicts (or 8-way if only 8 threads per warp). This is a well-known issue. Solutions: 1. Use a transpose or swizzled layout for SMEM when doing float4 reads. 2. Use [e, l] layout instead, so that reading e as float4 means reading contiguous memory for one e and varying l... but then we want to read e contiguously. Actually, for accumulation, we want to read `smem_depthwise[l, e]` for a fixed l and varying e. With layout [l, e], varying e means strided access. This is bad for vectorization. Alternative: use [E_TILE_PAD/4, l] with each entry being a float4, or use a blocked layout. This is getting complex. Let me skip float4 for SMEM and just use scalar loads. Kernel structure: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> inline __device__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865475f)); } template <int L_TILE, int E_TILE> __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ pw1, const float* __restrict__ dw, const float* __restrict__ pw2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K ) { const int b = blockIdx.x; const int l_block = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B) return; const int P = K / 2; const int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; const int l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L); const int l_count = l_end - l_start; const int l_win_start = l_start - P; const int l_win_count = l_count + K - 1; const int E_PAD = E_TILE + 1; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_pw1 = smem; float* smem_act = smem + E_TILE * l_win_count; const int num_out = C * l_count; const int out_per_thread = (num_out + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int out_start = threadIdx.x * out_per_thread; const int out_end = min(out_start + out_per_thread, num_out); const int out_n = out_end - out_start; float acc[128 / (512/256)]; // adjust based on L_TILE and blockDim // Actually for generality, use a fixed max or allocate based on compile-time known max ``` Wait, I can't have variable-length arrays in CUDA, and even fixed-size arrays need the size to be a compile-time constant if I want to be safe. This is excellent for cache performance. One issue: the condition `if (l_actuals[i] >= 0 && l_actuals[i] < L)` in the inner loop causes divergence. For boundary blocks, some l_actuals might be out of bounds. But the cost is small (just one comparison per 17 elements per c). Actually, it's inside the c loop, so 256 * 17 = 4352 comparisons. The compiler can optimize some of this. For full blocks (not at sequence boundaries), all l_actuals are valid. The compiler might not know this at compile time unless we make it a template or use assertions. But with L=4096 and L_TILE=128, most blocks are full. For the first block, l_actuals = -4 to 12. Some are negative. For the last block, l_actuals = 4028 to 4044. All valid. To optimize, we can compute the valid range once: ```c int valid_start = max(0, l_win_start + l_win_base); int valid_end = min(L, l_win_start + l_win_base + 17); ``` Then in the c loop, only iterate i where l_actuals[i] is valid. But the valid region is contiguous. So we can unroll the loop with bounds checks outside: ```c int l0 = l_win_start + l_win_base; int num_valid = 17; int valid_offset = 0; if (l0 < 0) { valid_offset = -l0; num_valid -= -l0; } if (l0 + 17 > L) num_valid -= (l0 + 17 - L); for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float w = pw1[...]; if (valid_offset == 0 && num_valid == 17) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 17; i++) pw1_sums[i] += w * input[...]; } else { for (int i = valid_offset; i < valid_offset + num_valid; i++) { pw1_sums[i] += w * input[...]; } } } ``` This is getting complex. For simplicity, I'll keep the if check inside and trust the compiler. Or I can manually set x=0 for out-of-bounds positions. Let me write it cleanly: ```c for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float w = pw1[(e_base + e_local_base) * C + c]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 17; i++) { int l_act = l_actuals[i]; float x = (l_act >= 0 && l_act < L) ? input[((b * C + c) * L + l_act)] : 0.0f; pw1_sums[i] += w * x; } } ``` The ternary operator is branchless on modern GPUs. For project_back accumulation, can we also restructure to reuse pw2 loads across multiple l positions? Threads in the block are assigned as: - out_start = threadIdx.x * 64 - For thread t: handles output out_idx from t*64 to t*64+63. - c = out_idx / 128, l = out_idx % 128. Thread 0: c=0, l=0..63 Thread 1: c=0, l=64..127 Thread 2: c=1, l=0..63 ... For threads 0 and 1: they both handle c=0, but l=0..63 and l=64..127. For each E_tile, we read pw2[c, e_base:e_base+E_TILE] and compute dot products with act[l, :] for each l. Same optimization: load pw2 slice once per c, reuse for all l in that c. ```c float pw2_slice[64]; int current_c = -1; for (int out_idx = out_start; out_idx < out_end; out_idx++) { int c = out_idx / l_count; int l = out_idx % l_count; if (c != current_c) { #pragma unroll for (int e = 0; e < E_TILE; e++) { pw2_slice[e] = pw2[(c * E + e_base + e)]; } current_c = c; } float sum = acc[out_idx - out_start]; #pragma unroll for (int e = 0; e < E_TILE; e++) { sum += pw2_slice[e] * smem_act[l * E_PAD + e]; } acc[out_idx - out_start] = sum; } ``` Since threads 0 and 1 have c=0, thread 0 loads pw2[0, :] and computes 64 dots. Thread 1 would also need pw2[0, :]... but they don't share registers. However, the pw2[0, :] load by thread 0 goes to L1 cache, and thread 1's load hits L1 (assuming they are in the same SM and the data is still hot). But to really optimize, we could restructure thread assignment so that ALL threads in a warp handle the SAME c and adjacent l positions. Currently threads 0 and 1 handle c=0 (good), threads 2 and 3 handle c=1, etc. Within a warp (32 threads): - Threads 0-1: c=0 - Threads 2-3: c=1 - Threads 4-5: c=2 - ... - Threads 30-31: c=15 So within a warp, there are 16 different c values. Each group of 2 threads handles one c. If we make each group of 4 threads handle one c, we'd have 8 c's per warp with each thread handling 32 l's. Not much difference. For L2 cache, thread 0 loads pw2[0, :], thread 2 loads pw2[1, :] (next row, contiguous in memory). These are coalesced if done simultaneously. So the loads are efficient. OK, I think the code is ready. Let me write it. One more optimization: since E=512, C=256, L=4096, B=32, K=9 are all known at compile time from the constructor (they're stored in the object), I can make the CUDA function take these as runtime parameters or I can hardcode them. Hardcoding allows better compiler optimization. To be general, I'll pass them as parameters but use template parameters for tile sizes. Actually, since `ModelNew` knows `channels`, `expanded_channels`, `kernel_size` at construction time, I could generate a kernel specialized for these sizes. But `load_inline` compiles at module load time, before we know the sizes. The sizes are only known at runtime when `__init__` is called. Unless I use runtime compilation with `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` in the constructor... but `load_inline` is typically called at module load. I'll use runtime parameters and hope the compiler optimizes the constant-size loops well. Wait, I can use `constexpr` or template parameters for the values that are constant within the problem (E_TILE, L_TILE), and pass the model dimensions as function args. Let me write the kernel with: ```c template <int L_TILE, int E_TILE> __global__ void kernel(...) ``` And instantiate it with L_TILE=128, E_TILE=64. Now, the kernel is getting long. Let me make sure the Python wrapper is correct. Python structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ ... CUDA code ... """ class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size): super().__init__() self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(channels, expanded_channels, 1) self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size, padding=kernel_size // 2, groups=expanded_channels) self.project_back = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, channels, 1) # Extract weights for CUDA kernel # Note: Conv1d weight shapes are [out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size] # For pointwise: [expanded_channels, channels, 1] # For depthwise: [expanded_channels, 1, kernel_size] because groups=expanded_channels # For project_back: [channels, expanded_channels, 1] def forward(self, x): # Call CUDA kernel pass ``` Wait, nn.Conv1d weight shapes: - `pointwise.weight`: shape `(expanded_channels, channels, 1)` -> for CUDA I want `[expanded_channels, channels]` - `depthwise.weight`: shape `(expanded_channels, 1, kernel_size)` -> for CUDA I want `[expanded_channels, kernel_size]` - `project_back.weight`: shape `(channels, expanded_channels, 1)` -> for CUDA I want `[channels, expanded_channels]` I can pass the weights directly by squeezing the last dimension. Actually, in CUDA I should align with PyTorch's memory layout. PyTorch Conv1d weights are stored as `[out_channels, in_channels / groups, kernel_size]`. So: - pw1: [E, C, 1]. Squeezing gives [E, C]. Memory layout: C is contiguous dimension. - dw: [E, 1, K]. Squeezing gives [E, K]. Memory layout: K is contiguous. - pw2: [C, E, 1]. Squeezing gives [C, E]. Memory layout: E is contiguous. So in CUDA: - `pw1[e, c]` is at `e * C + c` - `dw[e, k]` is at `e * K + k` - `pw2[c, e]` is at `c * E + e` This matches my kernel code! For the GELU exactness: PyTorch's nn.GELU in fp32 might use a different algorithm than CUDA's `erff`. Let me check. PyTorch's GELU source code uses a hardcoded polynomial approximation or `erf`. On CUDA, it calls `erf` from cuMath. In fp32, `torch.nn.GELU` and `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` should match very closely. Actually, PyTorch's GELU implementation has an `approximate` parameter. Default is `none`, which uses `erf`. The CUDA implementation likely uses `erff()`. To be safe, let me run a quick test after compiling to check numerical equivalence. One thing I noticed earlier: for `nn.GELU`, in fp32, `erff` should give results within 1e-7 typically. The fp32 tolerance is 1e-4, so we're very safe. Now let me write the full kernel. Simpler kernel design: - Grid: (batch, block_l), where each block processes L_TILE positions. - Within each block, we have threads. Each thread computes a subset of (C_out × L_out) output values. - We need to perform the whole fused computation inside shared memory. Since E=512, L=4096. The memory footprint: - Input [B, C, L] = 32 * 256 * 4096 * 4 = 128 MB - Intermediate after pointwise [B, E, L] = 32 * 512 * 4096 * 4 = 256 MB (too large for shared memory) - After depthwise+GELU [B, E, L] = 256 MB - Output [B, C, L] = 128 MB Shared memory per block is limited to 227KB. We cannot hold the full intermediates. We need to tile over E and L. So tile over both E and L. For each (L_TILE, E_TILE): 1. Load necessary input window for pointwise and compute pointwise -> store E_TILE * L_win in shared memory. 2. Apply depthwise on those E_TILE channels for the L_TILE output positions, using K depth weights. Read from first shared mem, produce second shared mem (after GELU). 3. Accumulate into output registers (C x L_TILE per thread, but too much). Actually each thread can accumulate for its assigned output positions. Given 512 threads per block, and L_TILE maybe 64, E_TILE maybe 32. Each block processes l in [l_start, l_start+L_TILE). - Need input l in [l_start - P, l_start + L_TILE - 1 + P], count = L_TILE + K - 1. - Shared size for pw1 output: E_TILE * (L_TILE + K - 1) * 4 bytes. - Shared size for dw+gelu output: E_TILE * L_TILE * 4 bytes. Let's choose E_TILE = 32, L_TILE = 64. SMEM = 32 * (64+8) * 4 + 32 * 64 * 4 = 32 * 72 * 4 + 8192 = 9216 + 8192 = 17408 bytes. Very small. We could increase to E_TILE = 64, L_TILE = 64: SMEM = 64 * 72 * 4 + 64 * 64 * 4 = 18432 + 16384 = 34816 bytes. Still small. Even E_TILE = 128, L_TILE = 64: SMEM = 128 * 72 * 4 + 128 * 64 * 4 = 36864 + 32768 = 69632 bytes. Good. E_TILE = 128, L_TILE = 128: SMEM = 128 * (128+8) * 4 + 128 * 128 * 4 = 128*136*4 + 65536 = 69632 + 65536 = 135168 bytes. Good. E_TILE = 256, L_TILE = 128 > 227KB? SMEM = 256*136*4+256*128*4 = 139264+131072 = 270336 > 227KB. So too big. Let's try E_TILE = 128, L_TILE = 128. SMEM = 135KB. Plus bias registers? Bias can be kept in registers or loaded directly. Number of output elements per block: C * L_TILE = 256 * 128 = 32768. With 512 threads, each thread handles ~64 output values. This seems fine. Total blocks: B * (L / L_TILE) = 32 * 32 = 1024 blocks. This provides good occupancy. Now let's write the kernel more carefully. Variables: - `pw1_weight` shape [E, C] (after squeeze) - `pw1_bias` shape [E] - `dw_weight` shape [E, K] - `dw_bias` shape [E] - `pw2_weight` shape [C, E] - `pw2_bias` shape [C] But note: PyTorch Conv1d weight shapes: - pointwise: [E, C, 1], squeeze => [E, C] - depthwise: [E, 1, K]. groups=E, squeeze => [E, K] - project_back: [C, E, 1], squeeze => [C, E] We need to add bias terms. Kernel logic per block (b, l_block): ```cpp // Constants: constexpr int L_TILE = 128; constexpr int E_TILE = 128; // thread assignment for output: // Each thread handles a contiguous chunk of (C * L_TILE) outputs. int out_total = C * L_TILE; int out_per_thread = (out_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int out_start = tid * out_per_thread; int out_end = min(out_start + out_per_thread, out_total); // local accumulator for each output assigned to this thread. // Since out_per_thread <= (32768+511)/512 ≈ 64, use an array in registers. float acc[64]; for i=0; i<64; ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f; // We need to process over E_TILE tiles. int num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE; // PW1 phase: // For each e_tile, we need to compute smem_pw1[e_local, l_win] for e_local in [0, E_TILE), l_win in [0, L_WIN). // This is a matmul: [E_TILE] x [C] dot [C] x [L_WIN]. // We can parallelize over the E_TILE x L_WIN matrix. // Total elements in this tile: E_TILE * L_WIN. Usually 128*136=17408. // With 512 threads, each thread handles ~34 elements. // For each element, we sum over C (256). So pointwise1 cannot be computed per output point independently; we must share the pw1 computation across output channels via tiling. The simple 1D thread approach is terrible because each output point recomputes the entire intermediate tensor. Therefore, the tiling approach is necessary. Let's revisit the tiling approach. Grid: (B, ceil(L/L_TILE)) Block: (some 2D arrangement). Another idea: process all C output channels within a block. So output per block is [C, L_TILE]. We tile over E in chunks of E_TILE. For each e_tile: 1. Compute and store in shared memory `smem_pw1[E_TILE, L_WIN]`. 2. Compute and store in shared memory `smem_act[E_TILE, L_TILE]` (after DW+GELU). 3. Each thread accumulates `pw2_w[c, e] * smem_act[e, l]` into its output buffer for assigned (c, l). Step 1: `smem_pw1[e, l_win] = sum_c w1[e,c] * x[b,c,l_global] + b1[e]`. This is a GEMV for each e. Matrix W1 is [E_TILE, C], vector x is [C, L_WIN]. Result is [E_TILE, L_WIN]. To parallelize: - Use all threads. Number of threads = N. - Each thread handles a set of (e, l_win) pairs. - The total number of pairs is E_TILE * L_WIN. - `pairs_per_thread = (E_TILE * L_WIN + N - 1) / N`. - Thread `tid` handles pairs `tid*ppp` to `(tid+1)*ppp-1`. - For each pair (e_loc, l_loc): sum = 0 for c in [0, C): w = W1[e_global, c] x = input[b, c, l_global] sum += w * x smem_pw1[e_loc, l_loc] = sum + b1[e_global] This is straightforward. Let's draft the kernel in a third-person view, then code it. ```cpp template<int E_TILE, int L_TILE> __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ pw1_w, const float* __restrict__ pw1_b, const float* __restrict__ dw_w, const float* __restrict__ dw_b, const float* __restrict__ pw2_w, const float* __restrict__ pw2_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { const int b = blockIdx.x; const int l_block = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B) return; const int P = K / 2; const int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; const int l_end = min(l_start + L_TILE, L); const int l_count = l_end - l_start; const int L_WIN = l_count + K - 1; const int l_win_start = l_start - P; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_pw1 = smem; // [E_TILE][L_WIN] float* smem_act = smem + E_TILE * L_WIN; // [E_TILE][L_TILE] // --- Output assignment --- const int out_total = C * l_count; const int out_per_thread = (out_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int out_begin = threadIdx.x * out_per_thread; const int out_last = min(out_begin + out_per_thread, out_total); // We'll accumulate into a local array. Max size: 64. float acc[64]; for (int i = 0; i < (out_last - out_begin); ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f; const int num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE; for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < num_e_tiles; ++e_tile) { int e_base = e_tile * E_TILE; // --- Compute pointwise1 into smem_pw1 --- const int pw1_total = E_TILE * L_WIN; const int pw1_per_thread = (pw1_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int pw1_begin = threadIdx.x * pw1_per_thread; const int pw1_last = min(pw1_begin + pw1_per_thread, pw1_total); for (int idx = pw1_begin; idx < pw1_last; ++idx) { int e_local = idx / L_WIN; int l_local = idx % L_WIN; int e = e_base + e_local; int l_global = l_win_start + l_local; float sum = 0.0f; // loop over C if (e < E) { for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float w = pw1_w[e * C + c]; float x = (l_global >= 0 && l_global < L) ? input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global] : 0.0f; sum += w * x; } sum += pw1_b[e]; } smem_pw1[e_local * L_WIN + l_local] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // --- Compute depthwise + GELU into smem_act --- const int act_total = E_TILE * l_count; const int act_per_thread = (act_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; const int act_begin = threadIdx.x * act_per_thread; const int act_last = min(act_begin + act_per_thread, act_total); for (int idx = act_begin; idx < act_last; ++idx) { int e_local = idx / l_count; int l_local = idx % l_count; int e = e_base + e_local; float sum = 0.0f; if (e < E) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { sum += smem_pw1[e_local * L_WIN + (l_local + k)] * dw_w[e * K + k]; } sum += dw_b[e]; sum = gelu(sum); } smem_act[e_local * l_count + l_local] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // --- Accumulate project_back --- for (int out_idx = out_begin; out_idx < out_last; ++out_idx) { int c = out_idx / l_count; int l_local = out_idx % l_count; int e_local = threadIdx.x % E_TILE; // no, this doesn't work. // Actually we need to loop over e_local for this output point. // But each thread handles many outputs. For each output, we loop over E_TILE. // However, pw2_w[c, e] is constant for a given c across e_tiles. // We can load the pw2 weights into registers for this c. // But since this loop is over out_idx (max 64), and inner loop over E_TILE (32), // total iterations = 2048. That's okay. float sum = 0.0f; for (int e_local = 0; e_local < E_TILE && (e_base + e_local) < E; ++e_local) { sum += pw2_w[c * E + (e_base + e_local)] * smem_act[e_local * l_count + l_local]; } acc[out_idx - out_begin] += sum; } __syncthreads(); } // --- Write output with bias and residual --- for (int out_idx = out_begin; out_idx < out_last; ++out_idx) { int c = out_idx / l_count; int l_local = out_idx % l_count; int l_global = l_start + l_local; float val = acc[out_idx - out_begin] + pw2_b[c] + input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global]; output[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global] = val; } } ``` Wait, there is an issue! In the PW2 accumulation step, the `out_idx` loop is executed by all threads, but they have different ranges `out_begin..out_last`. This is fine. However, all threads read from `smem_act`. Shared memory is fast. But there is a potential issue: in the PW2 accumulation, I don't need a syncthreads after it. The syncthreads after it is for the next e-tile to reuse smem. That's correct. However, there is a performance issue: every thread loads `pw2_w[c * E + ...]` for every `out_idx` and every `e_local`. The weight `pw2_w` for a given c and e is loaded multiple times. But it's read from global memory. Since E=512 and C=256, total pw2 weights = 128K floats = 512KB. This fits in L2 cache easily. So repeated reads should be L2 hits. Fine. // max L_WIN padded\n\n // output assignment\n const int out_total = C * l_count;\n const int out_per_thread = (out_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n const int out_begin = threadIdx.x * out_per_thread;\n const int out_last = out_begin + out_per_thread < out_total ? out_begin + out_per_thread : out_total;\n const int my_n = out_last - out_begin;\n\n float acc[64];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 64; ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f;\n\n const int num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE;\n\n for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < num_e_tiles; ++e_tile) {\n int e_base = e_tile * E_TILE;\n\n // --- Pointwise 1 ---\n const int pw1_total = E_TILE * L_WIN;\n const int pw1_per_thread = (pw1_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n const int pw1_begin = threadIdx.x * pw1_per_thread;\n const int pw1_last = pw1_begin + pw1_per_thread < pw1_total ? pw1_begin + pw1_per_thread : pw1_total;\n\n for (int idx = pw1_begin; idx < pw1_last; ++idx) {\n int e_local = idx / L_WIN;\n int l_local = idx % L_WIN;\n int e_global = e_base + e_local;\n int l_global = l_win_start + l_local;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n if (e_global < E) {\n for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) {\n float w = pw1_w[e_global * C + c];\n float x = (l_global >= 0 && l_global < L) ? input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global] : 0.0f;\n sum += w * x;\n }\n sum += pw1_b[e_global];\n }\n smem_pw1[e_local * L_WIN + l_local] = sum;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // --- Depthwise + GELU ---\n const int act_total = E_TILE * l_count;\n const int act_per_thread = (act_total + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n const int act_begin = threadIdx.x * act_per_thread;\n const int act_last = act_begin + act_per_thread < act_total ? act_begin + act_per_thread : act_total;\n\n for (int idx = act_begin; idx < act_last; ++idx) {\n int e_local = idx / l_count;\n int l_local = idx % l_count;\n int e_global = e_base + e_local;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n if (e_global < E) {\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n sum += smem_pw1[e_local * L_WIN + (l_local + k)] * dw_w[e_global * K + k];\n }\n sum += dw_b[e_global];\n sum = gelu(sum);\n }\n smem_act[e_local * l_count + l_local] = sum;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // --- Project back accumulation ---\n for (int out_idx = out_begin; out_idx < out_last; ++out_idx) {\n int c = out_idx / l_count;\n int l_local = out_idx % l_count;\n float sum = acc[out_idx - out_begin];\n for (int e_local = 0; e_local < E_TILE && (e_base + e_local) < E; ++e_local) {\n sum += pw2_w[c * E + (e_base + e_local)] * smem_act[e_local * l_count + l_local];\n }\n acc[out_idx - out_begin] = sum;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n // --- Write output with bias and residual ---\n for (int out_idx = out_begin; out_idx < out_last; ++out_idx) {\n int c = out_idx / l_count;\n int l_local = out_idx % l_count;\n int l_global = l_start + l_local;\n float val = acc[out_idx - out_begin] + pw2_b[c] + input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global];\n output[((b * C + c) * L) + l_global] = val;\n }\n}\n\nextern \"C\"\nvoid fused_conv_gelu_res(\n const float* input,\n const float* pw1_w,\n const float* pw1_b,\n const float* dw_w,\n const float* dw_b,\n const float* pw2_w,\n const float* pw2_b,\n float* output,\n int B, int C, int E, int L, int K)\n{\n const int L_TILE = 128;\n const int E_TILE = 32;\n dim3 grid(B, (L + L_TILE - 1) / L_TILE);\n dim3 block(512);\n size_t smem_size = (E_TILE * (L_TILE + K - 1) + E_TILE * L_TILE) * sizeof(float);\n fused_conv_gelu_res_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(\n input, pw1_w, pw1_b, dw_w, dw_b, pw2_w, pw2_b, output,\n B, C, E, L, K\n );\n}\n\"\"\"\n\nfused_module = load_inline(\n name='fused_conv_gelu_res',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['fused_conv_gelu_res'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(channels, expanded_channels, 1)\n self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size,\n padding=kernel_size // 2, groups=expanded_channels)\n self.project_back = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, channels, 1)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n B, C, L = x.shape\n E = self.pointwise.out_channels\n K = self.depthwise.kernel_size[0]\n\n pw1_w = self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(-1)\n pw1_b = self.pointwise.bias\n dw_w = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1)\n dw_b = self.depthwise.bias\n pw2_w = self.project_back.weight.squeeze(-1)\n pw2_b = self.project_back.bias\n\n out = torch.empty_like(x)\n\n fused_module.fused_conv_gelu_res(\n x.contiguous(), pw1_w, pw1_b, dw_w, dw_b, pw2_w, pw2_b, out,\n B, C, E, L, K\n )\n return out\n" }

But each block uses 256 threads × 255 registers max = 65280 registers. If each thread uses ~60 registers (to keep occupancy high), that's 15360 registers per block = 60KB. We want multiple blocks per SM. If each thread loads 512 weights into registers, that's at least 512 register variables, which will definitely spill or use too many registers. OK, so loading all pw2 weights into registers is bad. Alternative: 1. Load pw2 weights into shared memory in tiles 2. Or have each thread read pw2 weights from global memory as it goes Option 2 is simple: for each e, read pw2_w[c, e] from global memory. Since c is fixed per thread and e iterates, this is a strided read but with cache, it might be OK since all threads with the same c (but different l) read the same pw2_w row. Actually wait, if thread layout is (c_local, l_local), then threads with the same c_local read the same pw2_w row. There are 4 such threads (for l_local = 0,1,2,3). If they iterate over e in lockstep, they'll read the same pw2_w[c, e] at the same time, which is broadcast from L1 cache. Very efficient! So we don't need pw2 weights in shared memory or registers at all. Just stream them from global memory. But we still need all threads to iterate over e in the same order for cache reuse. For the dot product: ```cuda float sum = pw2_b[c]; for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += smem_depthwise[e * 4 + l_local] * pw2_w[c * E + e]; } ``` Since all 4 threads with the same c_local read the same pw2_w values at each e, and smem_depthwise[e * 4 + l_local] for different l_local values are adjacent in shared memory, this is very efficient. The only issue: reading pw2_w sequentially might not be fully coalesced for different c values. Different warps read different c's, which are strided by E. But within a warp, if c values are the same, all threads read the same address (broadcast). Actually within a warp of 32 threads, if threadIdx.x is c_local and threadIdx.y is l_local, then threads (0,0), (0,1), (0,2), (0,3), (1,0)... are consecutive. A warp has 32 threads. If c_local ranges 0-63, then a warp covers 32 different c values. At each e, these 32 threads read pw2_w[c*E + e] for 32 different c's. These reads are strided by E = 512 floats = 2048 bytes. That's not coalesced - it's basically 32 different cache lines. But if E is large, each read for a fixed c is sequential over e, so the cache can prefetch the next e for that c. And since 32 threads read 32 different c's, the cache will load 32 cache lines. This isn't great for memory bandwidth. Alternative: Tile E so we process 16 e's at a time and load pw2_w[c_block*64 : c_block*64+64, e:e+16] into shared memory. That's 64*16 = 1024 floats = 4KB. Then each thread reads from shared memory. For the dot product, we process E in tiles of E_TILE: 1. Load pw2 weights for current c_block and e_tile into shared memory 2. Compute dot product for this tile 3. Move to next tile This is much better. Each block loads 4KB of pw2 weights once per E tile, and all threads in the block reuse them. Let me formalize with E_TILE = 16: Shared memory: 1. smem_input: 512 * 12 = 6144 floats 2. smem_depthwise: 512 * 4 = 2048 floats 3. smem_pw2: 64 * 16 = 1024 floats Total: 9216 floats = 36KB Compute per E tile: 1. Cooperative load: 64*16 pw2 weights (each thread loads 4) 2. Dot product: each thread does 16 mult-adds For E=512, there are 32 tiles. Each thread does 32 * 16 = 512 mult-adds total. Let me code this up: ```cuda #define E 512 #define C 256 #define K 9 #define L_TILE 4 #define C_TILE 64 #define E_TILE 16 #define P 4 __global__ void kernel(const float* input, const float* dw_w, const float* dw_b, const float* pw2_w, const float* pw2_b, float* output, int B, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_block = blockIdx.y; int l_block = blockIdx.z; int c_local = threadIdx.x; // 0..63 int l_local = threadIdx.y; // 0..3 int c = c_block * C_TILE + c_local; int l = l_block * L_TILE + l_local; if (b >= B || c >= C || l >= L) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; // E * (L_TILE + K - 1) float* smem_dw = smem_input + E * (L_TILE + K - 1); // E * L_TILE float* smem_pw2 = smem_dw + E * L_TILE; // C_TILE * E_TILE int l_win_start = l_block * L_TILE - P; int l_win_size = L_TILE + K - 1; // Load input window int input_total = E * l_win_size; for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; idx < input_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int e = idx / l_win_size; int l_off = idx % l_win_size; int l_global = l_win_start + l_off; float val = (l_global >= 0 && l_global < L) ? input[((b * E + e) * L) + l_global] : 0.0f; smem_input[e * l_win_size + l_off] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute depthwise + GELU int dw_total = E * L_TILE; for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; idx < dw_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int e = idx / L_TILE; int l_off = idx % L_TILE; float sum = dw_b[e]; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { sum += smem_input[e * l_win_size + (l_off + k)] * dw_w[e * K + k]; } smem_dw[e * L_TILE + l_off] = gelu(sum); } __syncthreads(); // Tiled pointwise2 float sum = pw2_b[c]; int num_e_tiles = E / E_TILE; // 32 for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < num_e_tiles; e_tile++) { // Load pw2 weights for this c_block and e_tile int pw2_total = C_TILE * E_TILE; for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; idx < pw2_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int c_l = idx / E_TILE; int e_l = idx % E_TILE; int c_g = c_block * C_TILE + c_l; int e_g = e_tile * E_TILE + e_l; smem_pw2[c_l * E_TILE + e_l] = (c_g < C && e_g < E) ? Because the 32 outputs at the same position share the same depthwise intermediates! Pointwise2 for different c's uses the same dw outputs. So computing dw once and reusing for all 32 c's is a huge win. Similarly, pw1 outputs at the 9 positions are shared by all 32 c's.
