Now, the question is whether the block-level parallelization is efficient. Let me think about the pw1 compute: For pw1 at 9 positions and 512 e's: 4608 GEMVs of size 256. 256 threads in a block. How to parallelize? Option A: 256 threads, each computes 18 GEMVs. Option B: parallelize each GEMV across threads. For a GEMV of W[e, :] dot x[:, l'], we can parallelize the dot product: - 256 threads can compute one GEMV together, each doing 256/256 = 1 multiply-add, then reduce. - But we have 4608 GEMVs. Only 256 at a time can be computed if we parallelize within one GEMV. - This would take 4608 / 256 = 18 sequential steps, with each step being a parallel dot of 256 elements + reduce. A segmented parallel reduction might be more efficient if we have enough blocks. But Option A (each thread does 18 GEMVs serially) means almost no inter-thread communication for pw1, which is simpler. Actually, for Option A, each thread needs to read 18 rows of W (each 256 floats). That's 18*256*4 = 18KB of global memory reads per thread. With 256 threads, that's 4.5MB of weight reads per block. But wait, the weights for pw1 are the same for all blocks with the same (b)! l doesn't affect pw1 weights. So for L=4096, all 4096 blocks for the same b read the same pw1 weights. With L2 cache, after the first few blocks, weights are cached. Actually, pw1 weights are [E, C] = [512, 256] = 131KB. That's tiny! It fits in L1 cache of one SM (192KB on H100). So weight reads are essentially free after the first read. GEMV compute: each of 18 GEMVs per thread needs to read x (256 floats) from shared memory. But all threads working on the same l' position share the same x. So x should be in shared memory. Let me design the pw1 computation: 1. Load input(b, 0:C, l'-4) to (l'+4) into smem_input[C × 9] = 2304 floats 2. Each thread handles a subset of (e, l') pairs: - Thread i handles e from i*2 to i*2+1? No, we want better memory access. - Thread i handles e = i % 512 (but there are only 256 threads) - Actually, each thread handles 2 e's: e = threadIdx.x and e = threadIdx.x + 256 - For each e, the thread computes pw1 for all 9 l' positions. Wait, if thread 0 computes e=0 and e=256 for all 9 l' positions: - It needs to read pw1_w[0, :] and pw1_w[256, :] - For each l' position, it reads x[:, l'] from smem and computes dot products. pw1_w[0, :] and pw1_w[256, :] are at different memory addresses. If they're in L2 cache, reading them is fast. But 9 * 2 * 256 = 4608 multiply-adds per thread. At 1 FLOP per cycle, that's 4608 cycles. At 2GHz, that's 2.3 microseconds. Plus memory access. Actually, H100 Tensor Cores can do matmul much faster. But for a small GEMV like 512×256, there's overhead in using Tensor Cores. Regular FP32 ALUs might be fine. But wait, there's a better way. The pw1 is a matrix multiplication of [512, 256] × [256, 9] if we view it as all 9 positions together. This is a small GEMM. If we could use cuBLAS, it would be efficient. But within a CUDA kernel, we don't have easy access to cuBLAS. We could call it from the host, but that's an extra kernel launch. If we do pw1 on the host via PyTorch, we get this optimization for free. So the partial fusion strategy: 1. PyTorch: pw1 (uses cuBLAS/cuDNN, very efficient) 2. Custom kernel: dw + gelu + pw2 + residual Now the custom kernel input is [B, 512, L], and it needs to output [B, 256, L]. The work per output is: - For each (c, l): dot over E=512 of pw2_w[c,e] * GELU(dw(e, l)) - dw(e, l) needs K inputs at the same e For a block handling C_TILE=32 channels and L_TILE=4 positions: - Need dw at 512×4 = 2048 positions - Each dw needs K inputs from pw1 output - pw1 input is [B, 512, L] in global memory Cache behavior: for one block at (b, l_block), we read pw1 at [b, :, l_block*4-4 : l_block*4+8]. That's 512×12 = 6144 floats. L2 cache can hold this, and adjacent blocks share some of it. Computing dw for 2048 positions with 256 threads: each thread computes 8 dw outputs. Each dw output: 9 multiply-adds + GELU. Very simple. Then pointwise2 for 128 outputs (32 channels × 4 positions) with 256 threads: each thread handles 0.5 outputs? No, better to have each thread handle one output and use 128 threads, or have each thread handle half the E dimension. Actually for pointwise2: each of 128 outputs needs a dot over E=512. With 256 threads, we can have 2 threads cooperate on one output, each doing 256 multiplies. Or have 256 threads each contribute to a subset and reduce. Simpler: each thread handles one output. But there are only 128 outputs and 256 threads. Half the threads are idle. Better: C_TILE=64, L_TILE=4 → 256 outputs, exactly matching 256 threads! Wait, but earlier I said 256 threads per block and 64 channels × 4 positions = 256 outputs. Each thread handles exactly 1 output. For dw: need 512×4 = 2048 dw outputs. 256 threads, each computes 8 dw outputs. For pw2: 256 outputs, each needs 512 multiplies = 131072 multiplies total. 256 threads each do 512 multiplies. Total work per thread: 8*9 + 512 = 584 FLOPs. Plus memory. Very light! But the issue: pw2 weights. Each output channel needs 512 weights. If we have 64 channels per block, we need to load 64*512 = 32768 weights. That's 128KB. We can tile E: process 16 e's at a time. Each E tile: 1. Load pw2 weights for 64 channels × 16 e's = 1024 floats into smem 2. Threads read smem_pw2 and smem_dw to compute partial sums 3. Accumulate For dw: compute all 512×4 = 2048 dw outputs first, store in smem_dw (2048*4=8KB). Then do tiled pw2. Shared memory: - smem_input: 512 × 12 = 6144 floats = 24KB (input window for dw) - smem_dw: 512 × 4 = 2048 floats = 8KB - smem_pw2: 64 × 16 = 1024 floats = 4KB Total: 36KB Grid: B × (C/64) × (L/4) = 32 × 4 × 1024 = 131072 blocks This seems very efficient and relatively simple to implement. Wait, but I don't know C at compile time. The C and E dimensions are constructor arguments. In the reference, C = channels = 256, E = expanded_channels = 512. But they're passed as arguments. For generality, I should write the kernel to handle arbitrary C, E, K, but given the compilation issues, I might need to hardcode for the test case or use a slightly parameterized approach. I'll keep it simple and avoid any complex thread mapping. One more optimization: for dw, instead of computing all E×L_TILE and storing, I can tile E too. For each E_TILE: 1. Load pw1 input for E_TILE channels × l_window into smem 2. Compute dw for E_TILE × L_TILE, apply GELU 3. Load pw2 weights for C_TILE × E_TILE into smem 4. Threads accumulate partial dot products This way smem_input is smaller: E_TILE × l_win instead of E × l_win. For E_TILE = 64: - smem_input: 64 × 12 = 768 floats = 3KB - smem_dw: 64 × 4 = 256 floats = 1KB - smem_pw2: 64 × 16 = 1024 floats = 4KB Total: 8KB E tiles: 512/64 = 8 tiles. Per tile: - Load smem_input: all threads load part of 768 elements - Compute dw: 256 threads for 256 elements = 1 per thread - Load smem_pw2: all threads load part of 1024 elements - Dot product: each of 256 outputs needs 16 mult-adds from smem This is very clean! Let me finalize. Grid: B × (C/C_TILE) × (L/L_TILE) Block: (C_TILE, L_TILE/TBD) Wait, I want 256 threads per block. With C_TILE=64 and L_TILE=4, total outputs = 256. Each thread handles 1 output. Block = 1D of 256 threads, or 2D. Mapping threadIdx.x to (c_local, l_local): - c_local = threadIdx.x % C_TILE - l_local = threadIdx.x / C_TILE - This gives c_local in [0, 63], l_local in [0, 3] Now for the E-tile version: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(const float* pw1_out, const float* x_original, const float* dw_w, const float* dw_b, const float* pw2_w, const float* pw2_b, float* output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { const int P = K / 2; const int C_TILE = 64; const int L_TILE = 4; const int E_TILE = 64; const int PW2_E_TILE = 16; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_block = blockIdx.y; int l_block = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int c_local = tid % C_TILE; int l_local = tid / C_TILE; int c = c_block * C_TILE + c_local; int l = l_block * L_TILE + l_local; if (b >= B || c >= C || l >= L) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; // E_TILE * l_win float* smem_dw = smem_input + E_TILE * (L_TILE + K - 1); // E_TILE * L_TILE float* smem_pw2 = smem_dw + E_TILE * L_TILE; // C_TILE * PW2_E_TILE int l_win_start = l_block * L_TILE - P; int l_win_size = L_TILE + K - 1; float sum = pw2_b[c]; int num_e_tiles = (E + E_TILE - 1) / E_TILE; for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < num_e_tiles; e_tile++) { int e_base = e_tile * E_TILE; // Load pw1 input for this E tile int input_total = E_TILE * l_win_size; for (int idx = tid; idx < input_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int e_l = idx / l_win_size; int l_l = idx % l_win_size; int e_g = e_base + e_l; int l_g = l_win_start + l_l; float val = (e_g < E && l_g >= 0 && l_g < L) ? pw1_out[((b * E + e_g) * L) + l_g] : 0.0f; smem_input[e_l * l_win_size + l_l] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute depthwise + GELU for this E tile int dw_total = E_TILE * L_TILE; for (int idx = tid; idx < dw_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int e_l = idx / L_TILE; int l_l = idx % L_TILE; int e_g = e_base + e_l; float dw_sum = (e_g < E) ? dw_b[e_g] : 0.0f; if (e_g < E) { for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw_sum += smem_input[e_l * l_win_size + (l_l + k)] * dw_w[e_g * K + k]; } smem_dw[e_l * L_TILE + l_l] = gelu(dw_sum); } else { smem_dw[e_l * L_TILE + l_l] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Tiled pointwise2 int num_pw2_tiles = (E_TILE + PW2_E_TILE - 1) / PW2_E_TILE; for (int p_tile = 0; p_tile < num_pw2_tiles; p_tile++) { int p_base = p_tile * PW2_E_TILE; // Load pw2 weights int pw2_total = C_TILE * PW2_E_TILE; for (int idx = tid; idx < pw2_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_l = idx / PW2_E_TILE; int e_l = idx % PW2_E_TILE; int c_g = c_block * C_TILE + c_l; int e_g = e_base + p_base + e_l; smem_pw2[c_l * PW2_E_TILE + e_l] = (c_g < C && e_g < E) ? pw2_w[c_g * E + e_g] : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread accumulates for its output for (int e_l = 0; e_l < PW2_E_TILE && (p_base + e_l) < E_TILE && (e_base + p_base + e_l) < E; e_l++) { sum += smem_pw2[c_local * PW2_E_TILE + e_l] * smem_dw[(p_base + e_l) * L_TILE + l_local]; } __syncthreads(); } } // Add residual and write if (l < L && c < C) { float res = x_original[((b * C + c) * L) + l]; output[((b * C + c) * L) + l] = sum + res; } } ``` Wait, the residual is x (original input), which has shape [B, C, L]. For the partial fusion approach: 1. pw1_out = self.pointwise(x) # [B, E, L] 2. fused_kernel(pw1_out, x, dw_w, dw_b, pw2_w, pw2_b, output, ...) This looks much simpler and should compile quickly. Let me estimate performance: - Memory reads per block: - pw1 input: 64 * 12 * 4 bytes (with caching, actually less due to overlap with adjacent blocks) - pw2 weights: 64 * 64 * 4 bytes = 16KB per E tile - x residual: 64 * 4 * 4 bytes = 1KB - output write: 64 * 4 * 4 bytes = 1KB - Total per block: ~4KB (from pw1 input in cache) + 16KB (pw2 weights in cache) + 2KB = 22KB - Number of blocks: 131072 - Total memory traffic: ~2.9 GB At 3.35 TB/s: ~0.86 ms. The reference uses ~2.28 GB of memory traffic (from earlier calculation), so we have a bit more total memory movement but much better locality (mostly cached weights). Plus fewer kernel launches. Actually, the biggest issue with partial fusion is that pw1 uses tensor cores and is very fast. The remaining operations (dw + gelu + pw2 + residual) account for most of the memory traffic in the reference. In the reference: - pw1: reads x [33.5M], writes out1 [67M] → 100.5M floats - dw: reads out1 [67M], writes out2 [67M] → 134M floats - gelu: reads out2 [67M], writes out3 [67M] → 134M floats - pw2: reads out3 [67M], writes out4 [33.5M] → 100.5M floats - add: reads x + out4 [67M], writes out [33.5M] → 100.5M floats My partial fusion: - pw1: 100.5M floats - custom: reads pw1_out [67M] + x [33.5M], writes output [33.5M] = 134M floats Total: 234.5M floats = 938 MB → 0.28 ms at 3.35 TB/s. Standard fast GELU: ```cuda __device__ inline float fast_gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } ``` Or even simpler, approximate with: ```cuda __device__ inline float fast_gelu(float x) { float c = x * 0.044715f * x * x; float tanh_arg = 0.7978845608f * (x + c); return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(tanh_arg)); } ``` But GELU is relatively fast compared to the memory operations and GEMV compute. Implementation details: - For pw1: process in C tiles to avoid loading all pw1_w weights at once. - Tile C into C_TILE=64. - For each C tile: - Load x for C_TILE channels into smem (already there) - Load pw1_w for E_TILE=64 channels and C_TILE=64 into registers or smem - Each thread computes pw1 for some e's Actually, I don't need to tile C for pw1 if I stream C and accumulate in registers. For one pw1 output (e, l'): - It's a dot product over c in [0, 255] of pw1_w[e, c] * x[c, l']. - If we tile C into 4 tiles of 64: - For each tile, load x[64] into smem (already done), load pw1_w[e, c_tile:c_tile+64] into registers. - Compute partial dot product. - Accumulate. Since pw1_w for each e is 256 floats = 1KB. For 24 e's per thread: 24KB of weights. That's a lot of register pressure. Alternative: don't load pw1_w into registers. Stream from global memory: - For each c in [0, 255]: - Read x[c, l'] from smem. - Read pw1_w[e, c] from global memory. - Multiply and accumulate. If all 256 threads access the same c but different e's, then x[c, l'] is broadcast from smem, and pw1_w[:, c] is read by all threads. pw1_w is stored as [E, C]. Reading pw1_w[e, c] for different e but same c means reading from the same column. This is NOT coalesced - it's strided by E=512 floats = 2048 bytes. This would be very slow! 256 threads reading the same column with a 2048-byte stride means 256 cache lines per c value. That's 256 × 64 = 16KB read for just 1KB of useful data. Terrible bandwidth. To fix this, we need to read pw1_w in COALESCED manner. That means reading rows, not columns. If each thread handles multiple e's, say 8 e's: - Thread i reads pw1_w[i*8 : i*8+8, c] for each c. - For fixed c, thread i reads 8 consecutive floats starting at (i*8)*C + c*1 in row-major [E, C] layout. - Wait, pw1_w[e, c] is at index e*C + c. - For consecutive e's and fixed c: e*C + c, (e+1)*C + c. These are C floats apart = 1024 bytes apart for C=256. Not consecutive! Oh right, in PyTorch nn.Conv1d weights, the shape is (out_channels, in_channels, 1). The memory layout is contiguous in in_channels first, then out_channels. So weight[e, c] and weight[e+1, c] are C apart. To read coalesced for fixed e and varying c: that's accessing consecutive memory for threads with consecutive c. For pw1 computation: - Each thread computes pw1 for a fixed set of e's and all l's in the window. - For one pw1 output (e, l'), the dot product over c can be parallelized across threads. Actually, for a better approach, parallelize the dot product across threads: - Thread i computes dot(x[:, l'], pw1_w[e, :]) partially for c in [i*C/threads, (i+1)*C/threads). - Then reduce among threads. But reduction requires synchronization. And we have 512 e's × 12 l's = 6144 dot products. If we parallelize each dot product across 256 threads, we'd need 6144 reductions. That would be very slow with __syncthreads(). So reduction is not the way to go. Alternative layout: transpose pw1 weights so they're [C, E] instead of [E, C]. - Then pw1_w[c, e] and pw1_w[c+1, e] are consecutive. - For a dot product over c, if threads read the same e but different c, they access consecutive memory. That's coalesced! In PyTorch, nn.Conv1d weights are (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). So for pointwise, it's (E, C, 1). To get (C, E), I'd need to transpose them before passing to the kernel. That's easy to do in Python. Let me plan to transpose the weights: - pw1_w_t = pw1_w.t() # [C, E] - Then in the kernel, pw1_w_t[c, e] gives the weight for input c, output e. For coalesced access: - All threads in a warp reading pw1_w_t[c, e] for the same e and consecutive c: perfectly coalesced! - Each thread computes pw1 for a fixed e and all l positions in the window. - For c in [0, C-1]: read pw1_w_t[c, e] and x[c, l']. This is coalesced for a warp! - All threads in a warp reading for their respective e0 values read different columns, so they access different memory locations. But consecutive threads in a warp have consecutive e0 values, so they read columns e0, e0+1, ... These columns are separated by C floats. So threads in a warp read memory with a stride of C. This is NOT coalesced either! A warp of 32 threads reading pw1_w_t[c, e0], pw1_w_t[c, e0+1], etc. would each generate 32 different cache lines. Actually, the problem is that coalesced reads happen when consecutive threads access consecutive memory. For column-major layout [C, E], consecutive threads reading the same row but adjacent columns would read adjacent memory if columns are consecutive... wait, in row-major, pw1_w_t[c, e] is at c*E + e. So pw1_w_t[c, e+1] is at c*E + e + 1. These ARE consecutive! So if 32 threads in a warp read pw1_w_t[c, e0], pw1_w_t[c, e0+1], ..., pw1_w_t[c, e0+31] for the same c, that's perfectly coalesced! For the dot product over c: - All threads load x[c, l'] from smem (broadcast or shared). - Each thread loads pw1_w_t[c, thread_e] from global memory. - If thread_e are consecutive within a warp, and c is fixed for this step, then all threads in a warp access consecutive memory. Perfect! But each thread needs to sum over all c. So the loop over c goes: for c in 0..255: x_val = smem_x[c, l'] // broadcast from smem w_val = pw1_w_t[c, e] // coalesced for threads with consecutive e accum += x_val * w_val This is ideal! For a warp of 32 threads with consecutive e values: - At each c, threads read pw1_w_t[c, e:warp_e+31] → 32 consecutive floats. 1-2 cache lines. - x_val is broadcast from smem. What if I don't compute pw1 in the kernel, but instead pass the pw1 weights and compute it as the first step, then immediately do dw? The key is doing it cooperatively in a block. Let me try a much cleaner kernel design. I'll use a single block per (b, l_block) and process ALL c channels. The kernel will be cooperative and clean. ```cuda #define L_TILE 4 __global__ void kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l_block = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; const int P = K / 2; int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; int l_win_start = l_start - P; int l_win_size = L_TILE + K - 1; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x = smem; // C * l_win_size float* smem_pw1 = smem_x + C * l_win_size; // E * l_win_size float* smem_dw = smem_pw1 + E * l_win_size; // E * L_TILE // Load input window for (int idx = tid; idx < C * l_win_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / l_win_size; int l_off = idx % l_win_size; int l_g = l_win_start + l_off; smem_x[idx] = (l_g >= 0 && l_g < L) ? input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_g] : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Compute pw1 for all (e, l_win) for (int idx = tid; idx < E * l_win_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx / l_win_size; int l_off = idx % l_win_size; float sum = pw1_b[e]; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum += pw1_w_t[c * E + e] * smem_x[c * l_win_size + l_off]; } smem_pw1[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute dw + GELU for all (e, l_tile) for (int idx = tid; idx < E * L_TILE; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx / L_TILE; int l_off = idx % L_TILE; float sum = dw_b[e]; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { sum += smem_pw1[e * l_win_size + (l_off + k)] * dw_w[e * K + k]; } smem_dw[idx] = gelu(sum); } __syncthreads(); // Compute pw2 + residual for all (c, l_tile) for (int idx = tid; idx < C * L_TILE; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / L_TILE; int l_off = idx % L_TILE; int l_g = l_start + l_off; float sum = pw2_b[c]; for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += pw2_w[c * E + e] * smem_dw[e * L_TILE + l_off]; } output[((b * C + c) * L) + l_g] = sum + input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_g]; } } ``` This is extremely clean! The main loops are: 1. Load x: each thread handles (C*l_win_size)/blockDim.x elements 2. Compute pw1: each thread handles (E*l_win_size)/blockDim.x elements, each being a dot over C 3. Compute dw: each thread handles (E*L_TILE)/blockDim.x elements 4. Compute pw2: each thread handles (C*L_TILE)/blockDim.x elements, each being a dot over E For blockDim.x = 256: 1. C*l_win_size = 256*12 = 3072. Each thread loads 12 elements. 2. E*l_win_size = 512*12 = 6144. Each thread computes 24 pw1 outputs. 3. E*L_TILE = 512*4 = 2048. Each thread computes 8 dw outputs. 4. C*L_TILE = 256*4 = 1024. Each thread computes 4 pw2 outputs. Wait, step 4: with 256 threads and 1024 outputs, each thread computes 4 outputs. Each output is a dot over E=512. So each thread does 4*512 = 2048 multiply-adds. Total per thread: - Step 2: 24 × 256 = 6144 multiply-adds - Step 3: 8 × 9 = 72 multiply-adds - Step 4: 4 × 512 = 2048 multiply-adds Total: ~8K multiply-adds per thread. Shared memory: - smem_x: 3072 floats = 12KB - smem_pw1: 6144 floats = 24KB - smem_dw: 2048 floats = 8KB Total: 44KB Grid: B × L_blocks = 32 × 1024 = 32768 blocks. This kernel has NO reduction, NO complex thread mapping, just simple loops. It should compile easily and quickly. For pw1_w_t access pattern: pw1_w_t[c * E + e]. In the loop: for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum += pw1_w_t[c * E + e] * smem_x[...]; } For consecutive threads: - Thread i handles e_i = idx_i / l_win_size - For fixed c, thread i reads pw1_w_t[c * E + e_i] - If e_i are different (which they are for different threads), consecutive threads read pw1_w_t at the same c but different e. These are consecutive memory locations if e_i are consecutive. But threads are mapped by idx = tid, and idx increments by blockDim.x = 256. So thread 0 handles e=0, l=0; thread 1 handles e=0, l=1; etc. Wait! The mapping is: idx = tid + k * blockDim.x e = idx / l_win_size l_off = idx % l_win_size With l_win_size = 12: - idx=0: e=0, l=0 - idx=1: e=0, l=1 - ... - idx=11: e=0, l=11 - idx=12: e=1, l=0 So consecutive threads often have the same e but different l. When they read pw1_w_t[c*E + e], they're reading the SAME address for the same e. That's a broadcast! Not coalesced across different e's. To make it coalesced across e, we need threads with consecutive tid to have consecutive e. This means mapping e to tid directly, not interleaving l. A better mapping: for (int idx = tid; idx < E * l_win_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx % E; int l_off = idx / E; ... } With this mapping: - idx=0: e=0, l=0 - idx=1: e=1, l=0 - ... - idx=511: e=511, l=0 - idx=512: e=0, l=1 Now consecutive threads (within warp) have consecutive e for the same l. This is perfect for coalesced reads of pw1_w_t[c*E + e]! Yes! I'll use this mapping. Similarly for step 3 (dw): for (int idx = tid; idx < E * L_TILE; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx % E; int l_off = idx / E; ... } And step 4 (pw2 + residual): for (int idx = tid; idx < C * L_TILE; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx % C; int l_off = idx / C; ... } With this mapping, consecutive threads handle consecutive channels/expanded channels for the same position. This is ideal. For the pw2 computation in step 4, the loop is: for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += pw2_w[c * E + e] * smem_dw[e * L_TILE + l_off]; } For consecutive c and fixed l_off: - Thread i reads pw2_w[c_i * E + e] for varying e. - pw2_w is [C, E], row-major. pw2_w[c*E + e] and pw2_w[(c+1)*E + e] are E apart. - Consecutive threads with consecutive c access memory with stride E. NOT coalesced. To fix this, transpose pw2_w to [E, C] so pw2_w_t[e*C + c] gives the weight. Then: for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += pw2_w_t[e * C + c] * smem_dw[e * L_TILE + l_off]; } For consecutive c and fixed e: - Thread i reads pw2_w_t[e*C + c_i]. - These ARE consecutive memory locations! But the e loop is the outer loop, not parallelized. Within a warp, all threads iterate over e together. At each e, they read pw2_w_t[e*C + c_i] for their respective c_i. If c_i are consecutive, this IS coalesced. Yes! I'll transpose pw2_w too. Similarly for step 1 (loading x): for (int idx = tid; idx < C * l_win_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx % C; int l_off = idx / C; ... } With x in [B, C, L] layout (PyTorch default is NCHW for 3D, which is contiguous in C, L for fixed B... actually for Conv1d, PyTorch uses [B, C, L]). So x[b, c, l] is at ((b * C + c) * L + l). For fixed b and l, x[b, :, l] is contiguous in c. Wait, for step 1, we're loading x[b, c, l_win_start + l_off]. The mapping with c = idx % C means consecutive threads load consecutive c for the same l_off. Since x[b, c, l] is contiguous in c, this IS coalesced. Good. The mapping in step 2 uses pw1_w_t[c * E + e], but now with c as the loop variable: for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum += pw1_w_t[c * E + e] * smem_x[c * l_win_size + l_off]; } At each c, thread with e_i reads pw1_w_t[c * E + e_i]. If e_i are consecutive, this is coalesced. pw1_w_t is [C, E], so pw1_w_t[c*E + e] and pw1_w_t[c*E + e+1] ARE consecutive. Yes! Great, so with transposed weights and the right mapping, all memory accesses are coalesced or broadcast. Now I also need to handle the pointwise1 weight reading efficiently. Currently pw1_w_t is read from global memory in the inner loop of step 2. With 256 threads reading 256 different e's at each c, and repeating for 12 l_off values and C=256, total reads = 256 threads × 256 c × 12 l = 786,432 reads per block. But these are cached. Actually, 256 threads × 256 c = 65536 weight reads at each l_off. For 12 l_off values = 786,432 floats = 3MB per block. But wait - all threads with the same e read the SAME pw1_w_t[c, e] for all 12 l_off values. They compute 12 different dot products for the same e. But step 1 is the expensive part and needs all threads. Step 2 is a loop and each thread is independent. For step 1: we need all threads to compute pw1 for all E at l_win positions. We can do this cooperatively灵, with each thread handling multiple (e, l_win) pairs. For step 2: each thread computes dw and pw2 for its channel c at all 16 l positions. Actually, dw doesn't depend on c! All channels need the same dw outputs. So dw can also be computed cooperatively. Let me formalize: Step 1 (cooperative, all threads): Compute pw1 for all E × l_win positions. Each thread handles (E × l_win) / 256 pairs. For each pair: dot product over C. Step 2 (cooperative, all threads): Compute dw for all E × L_TILE positions. Each thread handles (E × L_TILE) / 256 pairs. For each pair: dot product over K. Step 3 (per thread, each handles one c): Compute pw2 for channel c at all L_TILE positions. Loop over l_local in [0, L_TILE-1]: - Dot product over E. - Add residual. - Write output. For step 3, with C=256 and blockDim=256, each thread handles exactly one c. Now for step 1 and step 2, the thread-to-work mapping should ideally have consecutive threads handle consecutive memory accesses. Step 1 mapping: for (int idx = tid; idx < E * l_win; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx % E; int l_off = idx / E; // compute pw1 } With l_win = L_TILE + K - 1 = 24. E * l_win = 512 * 24 = 12288. Each thread handles 12288 / 256 = 48 pairs. (Since 12288 is divisible by 256? 12288 / 256 = 48. Yes!) (8192 is divisible by 256? 8192 / 256 = 32. Yes!) Great, so the work is evenly divisible. For step 3, each thread computes 16 outputs (one channel, 16 positions). Each output needs 512 multiply-adds + add residual. Total: 8192 FMAs per thread. That's a significant amount. At 2 FMAs/cycle: 4096 cycles ≈ 2 μs. Step 1: each thread computes 48 pw1 outputs. Let me write it very cleanly. Also, I should note that pw1_w_t is transposed. In PyTorch: - pw1_w = self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(-1) # [E, C] - pw1_w_t = pw1_w.t().contiguous() # [C, E] Similarly for pw2_w. Actually, wait. When I transpose and make contiguous, it changes the layout but I need to make sure it works. Also, I need to handle the bias terms. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ inline float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865475f)); } __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ pw1_w_t, const float* __restrict__ pw1_b, const float* __restrict__ dw_w, const float* __restrict__ dw_b, const float* __restrict__ pw2_w_t, const float* __restrict__ pw2_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K ) { const int L_TILE = 16; const int P = K / 2; int b = blockIdx.x; int l_block = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int c = tid; // 0..255 int l_start = l_block * L_TILE; int l_end = l_start + L_TILE; if (l_end > L) l_end = L; int l_count = l_end - l_start; if (l_count <= 0) return; int l_win_start = l_start - P; int l_win_size = l_count + K - 1; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x = smem; float* smem_pw1 = smem_x + C * l_win_size; float* smem_dw = smem_pw1 + E * l_win_size; // Step 1: Load input window into shared memory int x_total = C * l_win_size; for (int idx = tid; idx < x_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_idx = idx / l_win_size; int l_off = idx % l_win_size; int l_g = l_win_start + l_off; float val = (l_g >= 0 && l_g < L) ? input[((b * C + c_idx) * L) + l_g] : 0.0f; smem_x[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: Compute pointwise1 for all E and l_win positions int pw1_total = E * l_win_size; for (int idx = tid; idx < pw1_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx % E; int l_off = idx / E; float sum = pw1_b[e]; for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < C; c_idx++) { sum += pw1_w_t[c_idx * E + e] * smem_x[c_idx * l_win_size + l_off]; } smem_pw1[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Step 3: Compute depthwise + GELU for all E and l_count positions int dw_total = E * l_count; for (int idx = tid; idx < dw_total; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx % E; int l_off = idx / E; float sum = dw_b[e]; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { sum += smem_pw1[e * l_win_size + (l_off + k)] * dw_w[e * K + k]; } smem_dw[e * l_count + l_off] = gelu(sum); } __syncthreads(); // Step 4: Compute pointwise2 + residual for this thread's channel if (c < C) { for (int l_off = 0; l_off < l_count; l_off++) { float sum = pw2_b[c]; for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += pw2_w_t[e * C + c] * smem_dw[e * l_count + l_off]; } int l_g = l_start + l_off; float res = input[((b * C + c) * L) + l_g]; output[((b * C + c) * L) + l_g] = sum + res; } } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with step 4. pw2_w_t[e * C + c] - if pw2_w is [C, E] originally and I transpose it, then pw2_w_t is [E, C]. But in PyTorch, nn.Conv1d weights are (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). For project_back, it's (C, E, 1). Squeezing gives (C, E). Transposing gives (E, C). In the kernel, for pointwise2, the weight for output channel c and input e is pw2_w[c, e]. After transpose, it's pw2_w_t[e, c]. So pw2_w_t[e * C + c] is correct. But wait, in step 4, the loop is: ```cuda for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { sum += pw2_w_t[e * C + c] * smem_dw[e * l_count + l_off]; } ``` For consecutive c values (threads in a warp), they read pw2_w_t[e*C + c] for the SAME e but DIFFERENT c. These are consecutive memory addresses! (e*C + c, e*C + c+1, etc.) And for the smem_dw read: consecutive threads read smem_dw[e*l_count + l_off] for the SAME e and l_off. That's a broadcast from shared memory. This is very efficient! The inner loop over e should be fast. However, each thread in a warp reads a different pw2_w_t value for each e. These reads go to L2 cache (since pw2 weights are the same across all blocks). With E=512 and C=256, pw2_w_t is 128KB. Multiple blocks can share this in cache. Great! One subtlety: PyTorch Conv1d computes output = conv(input, weight) + bias. The kernel should match this exactly. For pointwise1 (kernel=1), it's just a matrix-vector product per position: for each position l and output channel e: out[e, l] = sum_c input[c, l] * weight[e, c] + bias[e] For depthwise: for each e and l: out[e, l] = sum_k input[e, l + k - P] * weight[e, k] + bias[e] For project_back: same as pointwise1 but with C outputs.
